The Second Objection: Comparing Winter Equator to Summer in High Latitudes
Taftazani refutes the second objection about equatorial heat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why equatorial winter is not as hot as summer in high-latitude regions, despite similar solar distances.

Core Idea
Multiple factors—day length, habituation, and terrestrial causes—govern heat, not just solar distance.

The second objection compares equatorial winter (when the sun is farthest) to summer in a region at 47° latitude, claiming they are equally hot. Taftazani's refutation introduces additional causes: day length, habituation, and terrestrial factors. This lesson unpacks these alternative explanations and notes the author's own critique of the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection argues that the winter solstice at the equator (sun farthest) is as hot as summer in a high-latitude town like Saray.
The objection assumes distance from zenith is the only factor.
2
Examine the first part of the answer
Taftazani denies the similarity, pointing out that day length differs: high-latitude summers have very long days, while the equator has equal day and night year-round.
Day length difference: ~16 hours vs. 12 hours.
3
Consider habituation
Equatorial people are accustomed to heat, so their bodies are less affected. High-latitude people experience a sudden transition from cold to heat.
Habituation reduces perceived temperature.
4
Note the author's criticism
Taftazani notes that this answer is weak and that terrestrial causes also play a role.
Even the author admits the answer is not fully satisfactory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دائماً في المسامتة أو في القرب منها ، فتكون حرارتهم مفرطة، لأن قرب المسامتة في زمان يسير كما في الصيف عندنا ، مع تقدم برد الشتاء المخرج للهواء عن استعداد التسخن مسخن جداً فهذا أولى.
وجوابه : أن مسامتتهم لسرعة زوالها أقل نكاية وتسخيناً للهواء من المسامتة، أو القرب منها في البلاد ذوات العروض، لأن قرب المسامتة يبقى هناك أياماً كثيرة، ويكون النهار أطول من الليل طولاً ظاهراً. والسبب الدائم وإن ضعف، قد يكون أكثر تأثيراً من غير الدائم، وإن قوى كالحديد في نار لينة مدة، وفي نار قوية لحظة . وثانيهما أن زمان وصول الشمس إلى أول السرطان شتاء لخط الاستواء لكون الشمس على غاية البعد عن سمت رأسهم، وصيف البقعة عرضها سبعة وأربعون ضعف الميل الكلي كبلدة سراي لكونها على غاية القرب عنها مع أن بعدها عن سمت رأس البقعتين على السواء، فيكون حر شتاء خط الاستواء كحرّ صيف هذه البلدة، بل أكثر إذا تأملت لأن ما قبل هذه الحالة لهم من أسباب السخونة، ولأهل البلدة من أسباب البرودة، وإذا كان حر شتائهم هذا، فما ظنك بحر صيفهم .
وجوابه : منع تشابه حر الفصلين في البقعتين، وإنما يلزم لو انحصر سبب الحرّ في قرب الشمس من سمت الرأس وهو محال، فيجوز أن يشتد حرصيف البلدة المفروضة، بسبب تزايد طول النهار على الليل إلى الضعف تقريباً، لأن طول نهارها يبلغ ست عشرة ساعة تقريباً، وقصر لياليها ثماني ساعات كذلك، بخلاف خط الاستواء، فإن الليل والنهار فيه دائماً على السواء، فيتعادل الحر والبرد، وأيضاً المألوف لا يؤثر ، فلعل أهل خط الاستواء لإلفهم بالحر لا تتأثر أمزجتهم ، ولا تتسخن من حر مسامتة الشمس، ويستبردون الهواء عند بعد المسامتة ، أعني كون الشمس في الانقلابين، فيبقى الاعتدال بخلاف البلدة المفروضة، فإن الحر يشتد على جسمهم، ويؤثر فيهم لعدم الفهم به ، ولا نتقالهم إليه من شدة البرد، وإن كان على التدريج، ولا يخفي على المصنف ضعف هذا الجواب، وكذا إسناد حرّ البلدة إلى الأسباب الأرضية .
Always in zenith proximity or near it, so their heat is excessive, because proximity to the zenith in a short time as in our summer, preceded by winter cold that removes the air's readiness to heat, becomes very hot, so this is more likely. Its answer: Their zenith proximity due to its quick passing is less harmful and heating to the air than the zenith proximity or nearness in lands of latitudes, because the proximity to the zenith remains there many days, and the day is manifestly longer than the night. And the permanent cause, even if weak, may be more effective than the non-permanent, even if strong, like iron in a gentle fire for a while and in a strong fire for a moment. And secondly: The time of the sun's arrival at the beginning of Cancer is winter for the equator because the sun is at the utmost distance from their zenith, and summer for a locality whose latitude is forty-seven times the total declination, like the town of Saray, because it is at the utmost proximity to it, while the distance from the zenith of the two localities is equal. So the winter heat of the equator is like the summer heat of this town, even more if you consider, because before this state they have causes of heat, and the townspeople have causes of cold. And if their winter heat is like this, what do you think of their summer heat? Its answer: Preventing the similarity of the heat of the two seasons in the two localities. It would only follow if the cause of heat were confined to the nearness of the sun from the zenith, which is impossible. So it is possible that the summer heat of the supposed town becomes intense due to the increase of day length over night to nearly double, because its day length reaches about sixteen hours, and its night length about eight hours, unlike the equator, where night and day are always equal, so heat and cold are balanced. Also, what is familiar has no effect. Perhaps the people of the equator, due to their familiarity with heat, their temperaments are not affected, nor do they become heated from the heat of the sun's zenith proximity, and they consider the air cold when the zenith is distant – I mean when the sun is at the solstices – so balance remains, unlike the supposed town, where heat intensifies on their bodies and affects them due to unfamiliarity with it, and due to their transition to it from intense cold, even if gradual. And it is not hidden from the author the weakness of this answer, and likewise attributing the town's heat to terrestrial causes.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents two objections regarding the heat of equatorial regions and their refutations. The first objection claims that because the sun is always at or near the zenith at the equator, the heat there is excessive. The answer explains that the sun's passage through the zenith is very brief, while in regions at higher latitudes, the sun remains near the zenith for many days, causing greater cumulative heating. A permanent but weak cause (like a long exposure to moderate heat) can be more effective than a strong but brief one, like iron in a gentle fire for a long time versus a strong fire for an instant. The second objection compares winter at the equator (when the sun is farthest from the zenith) to summer at a latitude of 47° (like the town of Saray), arguing that the equatorial winter is as hot as that summer. The answer denies the similarity of the two seasons in the two locations. Even if the distance from the zenith is the same, the cause of heat is not limited to that distance; day length also matters. At high latitudes, days are much longer in summer (e.g., 16 hours of daylight) while nights are short, intensifying heat. At the equator, day and night are always equal, balancing heat and cold. Furthermore, habituation plays a role: equatorial people are accustomed to heat, so their bodies are less affected, whereas at higher latitudes, people experience a sudden transition from intense cold to heat, making it more impactful. The author notes that this answer is weak and attributes the heat to terrestrial causes.

الاعتدال
المألوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the sun is at the same angular distance, the heat must be the same regardless of location.
Correction: Heat depends on multiple factors including day length, habituation, and terrestrial causes, not just solar distance.

Checkpoint
What is one additional factor besides solar distance that explains why summer in high latitudes can be hotter than equatorial winter?
A) The sun is brighter in high latitudes.
B) Days are much longer in high-latitude summers.
C) The air is thinner at high latitudes.
D) There are more clouds at the equator.

Key Takeaway
Heat in a region is influenced by a combination of factors: solar distance, day length, habituation, and terrestrial conditions, not solely proximity to the sun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a spiritual 'heat' in your life—a sin or distraction that no longer shocks you because you have become accustomed to it. How does this lesson challenge you to break that familiarity and respond appropriately? What one concrete step will you take this week to recalibrate your sensitivity?
?
Consider a difficulty you are facing right now. Have you been attributing it to a single cause, ignoring the complexity of factors? What other influences—within you or outside—might be at play? How can acknowledging this lead to a more effective response today?
?
Taftazani admits the weakness of his own argument about habituation. Do you have the courage to admit when your own reasoning about your spiritual state is weak? What comfort do you find in the fact that human understanding is limited, and Allah knows all causes? Write a short prayer asking Allah to reveal the hidden factors in your life.

Debunking the Zenith Heat Claim
Response to a claim about heat at a hypothetical city.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that heat in a hypothetical city is due to longer days and lack of acclimatization, not the sun being at zenith.

Core Idea
Heat intensity depends on day length and acclimatization, not just solar zenith.

In this passage, the author addresses a claim that a certain city experiences intense heat because the sun is directly overhead at zenith. He offers an alternative explanation: the day is much longer than the night, and the inhabitants are not accustomed to such heat. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and notes the author’s own critique of this answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the author dismisses the zenith claim as impossible.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
The claim is that the city's intense heat is due to the sun being at zenith. Find where the author states this.
3
Understand the author's counter-arguments
The author gives two reasons: the day is much longer than the night, and the inhabitants are not acclimatised. Note how these differ from the zenith claim.
Compare with the equinox line example.
4
Consider the author's self-critique
The author admits his own answer is weak, and also questions attributing heat to earthly causes. Reflect on why he might say this.
This shows scholarly humility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في قرب الشمس من سمت الرأس وهو محال، فيجوز أن يشتد حرّ تلك البلدة المفروضة، بسبب تزايد طول النهار على الليل إلى الضعف تقريباً، لأن طول نهارها يبلغ ست عشرة ساعة تقريباً، وقصر لياليها ثماني ساعات كذلك، بخلاف خط الاستواء، فإن الليل والنهار فيه دائماً على السواء، فيتعادل الحر والبرد، وأيضاً المألوف لا يؤثر ، فلعل أهل خط الاستواء لإلفهم بالحر لا تتأثر أمزجتهم ، ولا تتسخن من حر مسامتة الشمس، ويستبردون الهواء عند بعد المسامتة ، أعني كون الشمس في الانقلابين، فيبقى الاعتدال بخلاف البلدة المفروضة، فإن الحر يشتد على جسمهم، ويؤثر فيهم لعدم الألف به ، ولا نتقالهم إليه من شدة البرد، وإن كان على التدريج، ولا يخفي على المصنف ضعف هذا الجواب، وكذا إسناد حرّ البلدة إلى الأسباب الأرضية . وأما الجواب عن احتجاج ابن سينا على كون سكان خط الاستواء أقرب الأصناف إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي، بالنظر إلى أوضاع العلويات، فهو أن تشابه الأحوال بمعنى أنه لا يطرأ عليهم تغير يعتد به ولا تلحقهم كذلك (٢) نكاية من حر أو برد لا يفيد المطلوب. أعني قربهم من الاعتدال الحقيقي الذي تساوي فيه(٣) الكيفيات لجواز أن يكون البالغ في الحرارة أو البرودة المألوفة كذلك
Regarding the sun being near the zenith, which is impossible, it is possible that the heat of that supposed city intensifies due to the increase of the length of day over night to nearly double, because its day length reaches about sixteen hours, and its nights are as short as eight hours, unlike the equinox line (khaṭṭ al-istiwāʾ), where night and day are always equal, so heat and cold are balanced. Also, what is familiar does not affect; perhaps the inhabitants of the equinox line, due to their familiarity with heat, their temperaments are not affected, nor do they become heated by the heat of the sun's zenith, and they find the air cold when the sun is distant from the zenith, meaning when the sun is at the solstices, so equilibrium remains, unlike the supposed city, where heat intensifies on their bodies and affects them due to lack of familiarity with it, and because they transition to it from severe cold, even if gradually. The author is not unaware of the weakness of this answer, and likewise attributing the city's heat to earthly causes. As for the answer to Ibn Sīnā's argument that the inhabitants of the equinox line are the closest species to true moderation, with respect to celestial configurations, it is that the similarity of conditions—meaning that no significant change befalls them, nor does any harm from heat or cold affect them—does not yield the desired conclusion, namely their nearness to true moderation in which qualities are equal, because it is possible that one who is extreme in heat or cold, yet accustomed, is likewise.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents two responses to arguments about heat and moderation. First, the author responds to a claim that heat in a hypothetical city is due to the sun being at zenith, arguing that it is actually due to longer days and acclimatization. He notes this answer is weak and that attributing heat to earthly causes is also problematic. Second, he responds to Ibn Sīnā's argument that equinox line dwellers are closest to true moderation because they experience no significant change. The author counters that lack of change does not imply true equality of qualities, as one could be accustomed to an extreme. The argument follows the order of objection and rebuttal, preserving the nuance that habituation can affect perception of moderation.

Misconception: The sun being directly overhead at zenith always causes the greatest heat.
Correction: The passage shows that other factors like day length and acclimatization are more significant.

Key Takeaway
Heat is not solely determined by the sun’s zenith position; day length and habituation play crucial roles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Ibn Sina's Moderation Argument Rebutted
Rebuttal of Ibn Sina's argument about equinox line moderation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why similarity of conditions does not entail true moderation in qualities.

Core Idea
Lack of change does not guarantee true equality of qualities; habituation can mask extremes.

The author now addresses Ibn Sina’s argument that inhabitants of the equinox line are closest to true moderation because they experience no significant changes in temperature. This lesson examines the author’s rebuttal that such stability does not prove the qualities are balanced, as one could be accustomed to constant heat or cold.

3
Understand the author's counter-argument
The author argues that similarity of conditions (no change) does not imply equality of qualities; one could be habituated to an extreme.
The key word is 'تشابه الأحوال' (similarity of conditions).
4

True moderation requires equal qualities, not just absence of change. Habituation can make an extreme seem normal.

Two Objections to the Fourth Climate Being Most Moderate
Two objections against the fourth climate's moderation claim and their answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two objections against the claim that the fourth climate is most moderate and their refutations based on the concepts of accidental vs true moderation and the role of intuition.

Core Idea
True moderation means equality of qualities, not just sufficient quantities.

This passage presents two criticisms of the widely held view that the fourth climate is the most moderate. The first objection confuses accidental moderation (sufficiency of qualities) with true moderation (equality of qualities). The second suggests that earthly causes, not celestial ones, could explain the abundance of perfections in the fourth climate. The author answers both using theological reasoning and intuition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overall understanding of the two objections and their answers.
Notice that the passage is structured as a claim, then two objections, then two answers.
2
Identify the two objections
Look for the words 'أحدهما' (the first) and 'ثانيهما' (the second) to locate each objection. Each is followed by an answer beginning with 'ودفعه' (its answer).
The first objection concerns accidental vs. true moderation; the second concerns earthly vs. celestial causes.
3
Understand the first objection and its answer
The objection claims that perfections follow accidental moderation, not true moderation. The answer refines this: the more something approaches true moderation (and the One Principle), the more it confers perfection.
Key terms: اعتدال عرضي (accidental moderation) and اعتدال حقيقي (true moderation).
4
Understand the second objection and its answer
The objection suggests that earthly causes might explain the lack of perfections at the equator and their abundance in the fourth climate. The answer uses intuition (حدس) to argue that it is impossible that no spot on the equator is free of earthly obstacles, and no town in the fourth climate is purely governed by celestial causes.
This demonstrates that earthly causes cannot fully account for the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب جمع من الأوائل وكثير من المتأخرين إلى أن أعدل الأصناف سكان الإقليم الرابع استدلالاً بالاثار كما هو مذكور في المتن، غني عن الشرح، وفيه إشارة إلى دفع اعتراضين : أحدهما : أن كثرة التوالد والتناسل وتوفر العمارات وغير ذلك من الكمالات إنما يتبع الاعتدال العرضي الذي هو توفر القسط الأليق من الكيفيات لا الحقيقي الذي هو تساويها ، وفيه النزاع. ودفعه أن المعتدل الفرضي كلما كان إلى المعتدل الحقيقي أقرب وبالواحد المبدأ أنسب كان بإفاضة الكمال أجدر ، فيتم الاستدلال بزيادة الكمال على زيادة القرب من الاعتدال الحقيقي على ما هو المطلوب . وثانيهما: أن قلة الكمالات في خط الاستواء وكثرتها في الاقليم الرابع . يجوز أن تكون عائدة إلى الأسباب الأرضية دون أوضاع العلويات. ودفعه أن الحدس يشهد بما ذكرنا ويحكم ببطلان أن لا يوجد في خط الاستواء، وهو أربعة آلاف فرسخ بقعة خالية عن الموانع الأرضية، ولا في الإقليم الرابع على كثرة بلادها، بلدة خالصة للأسباب العلوية .
A group of the ancients and many of the later scholars held that the most moderate of species are the inhabitants of the fourth climate, adducing by effects (āl-āthār) as mentioned in the text, which is sufficient without commentary. In it there is a reference to repelling two objections: the first: that the abundance of procreation, reproduction, availability of buildings, and other perfections only follows accidental moderation (al-iʿtidāl al-ʿaraḍī), which is the availability of the most suitable share of qualities, not true moderation (al-ḥaqīqī) which is their equality, and in this lies the dispute. Its answer: that the hypothetical moderate, whenever it is closer to the true moderate and more appropriate to the One Principle (al-wāḥid al-mabdaʾ), is more deserving of bestowing perfection; thus the argument is completed by the increase of perfection indicating increase of nearness to true moderation, which is the desired conclusion. The second: that the lack of perfections on the equinox line and their abundance in the fourth climate could be attributed to earthly causes without celestial configurations. Its answer: that intuition (al-ḥads) testifies to what we mentioned and deems it invalid that there is not found on the equinox line, which is four thousand parasangs, a spot free of earthly obstacles, nor in the fourth climate, despite its many lands, a town purely [governed] by celestial causes.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of two objections against the claim that the fourth climate is most moderate, followed by their answers. The author first states that a group of scholars hold this view based on observable effects. He then presents two objections. The first objection argues that the abundance of perfections in the fourth climate follows accidental moderation (sufficient quantity of qualities) not true moderation (equal quality). The answer refines this: the hypothetical moderate, the more it approaches true moderation and the One Principle, the more it confers perfection, so abundance of perfection indicates nearness to true moderation. The second objection suggests that the lack of perfections at the equator could be due to earthly causes, not celestial. The answer counters that intuition shows it is impossible that not a single spot on the four-thousand-parasang equator is free of earthly obstacles, nor is any town in the fourth climate purely governed by celestial causes, thus the argument from earthly causes fails.

Key Terms
اعتدال عرضي (iʿtidāl ʿaraḍī, accidental moderation)
اعتدال حقيقي (iʿtidāl ḥaqīqī, true moderation)
الواحد المبدأ (al-wāḥid al-mabdaʾ, the One Principle)
حدس (ḥads, intuition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any abundance of perfections indicates true moderation, but the passage distinguishes between accidental moderation (sufficiency of qualities) and true moderation (equality of qualities).
Correction: True moderation is about equality of qualities, not just having a sufficient quantity. The argument for the fourth climate's moderation relies on closeness to true moderation, not mere abundance.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first objection, what does the abundance of perfection indicate?
A) That the region has the most suitable accidental moderation.
B) That the region is closer to true moderation.
C) That the region is governed purely by celestial causes.
D) That the region has equal night and day.

Key Takeaway
The strongest argument for the fourth climate's moderation comes from its closeness to true balance, not mere abundance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you often settle for 'accidental moderation'—having enough of what you need—rather than seeking 'true moderation' where your qualities are balanced and aligned with your purpose? What would it take to shift towards that true balance?
?
The passage argues that closeness to the One Principle is what enables perfection. What are the 'earthly obstacles' in your life that prevent you from being purely guided by divine principles? Name one concrete step you can take this week to remove it.
?
If intuition (al-ḥads) testifies to a deeper truth beyond physical causes, how often do you trust that inner sense when it contradicts surface appearances? Reflect on a decision you are currently facing where you might be ignoring your intuition due to 'earthly' excuses.

Refuting the Claim of Equinox Moderation
Refutes the argument that the equinox line is more moderate because it lies on the boundary between north and south.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the equinox line is not considered moderate in Islamic geography, and how true moderation involves being between causes of heat and cold rather than geographic centrality.

Core Idea
True moderation is not about being the midpoint between poles; it's about being between the causes of heat and cold.

In this passage, Sa‘d al-Dīn al-Taftāzānī refutes an objection that the equinox line (the equator) should be more moderate than the fourth climate because it lies on the boundary between north and south. He clarifies that the moderation of climates depends on distance from the zenith and the extremes of heat and cold, not on simple geographic symmetry. He also provides a definition of the equinox line and its characteristics.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the objection presented in the first part of the passage. Notice how it argues from analogy: if being central makes the fourth climate moderate, then the equinox line should be even more moderate.
The objection is introduced with 'If it is said' (فإن قيل).
2
Understand the refutation
Focus on the author's reply ('We say'). He distinguishes two types of intermediateness: one between causes of heat and cold (zenith proximity), and another simply between poles. The equinox line falls under the latter, not the former, so it is not moderate.
The key phrase is 'التوسط ههنا توسط بين ما هو من أسباب الحرّ والبرد'.
3
Grasp the definition of the equinox line
Read the author's description of the equinox line. It is an imaginary line where day and night are always equal and the poles are on the horizon. This clarifies why the line is not moderate in the relevant sense.
The description includes the positions of the poles relative to the horizon.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that geographic symmetry guarantees moderation. The author shows that true moderation depends on the angle of the sun and the resulting temperature, not just latitude.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إذا صح الاستدلال على اعتدال الاقليم الرابع بكونه وسطاً بين الأقاليم بعيداً عن الفجاجة الشمالية والاحتراق الجنوبي، فأولى أن يستدل على اعتدال خط الاستواء بكونه على حاف الوسط من الشمال والجنوب. قلنا: التوسط ههنا توسط بين ما هو من أسباب الحرّ والبرد ـ أعني قرب المسامتة وبعدها بخلاف التوسط بين القطبين . فإنه نسبة الشمس إليها على السواء، وأهل ذلك الوسط دائماً في المسامتة ، أو القرب منها ، وإنما يصح الاستدلال لو كان غاية الحرّ والبرد تحت نقطتي الجنوب والشمال وليس كذلك. خط الاستواء : هو خط متوهم ابتداؤه من المشرق إلى المغرب تحت مدار رأس برج الحمل، والليل والنهار أبداً على ذلك الخط متساويان والقطبان هناك ملازمان للأفق ، أحدهما مما يلي مدار سهيل في الجنوب والاخر في الشمال مما يلي الجدي وهذا مثال ذلك خط قطب الجنوب ربع الجنوب ربع الشمال قطب الشمال ا الاستواء
If it is said: if the argument for the moderation of the fourth climate by its being central between the climates, far from northern harshness and southern burning, is valid, then it is more fitting to argue for the moderation of the equinox line because it is on the edge of the middle between north and south. We say: the intermediateness here is an intermediateness between what is among the causes of heat and cold—namely nearness to zenith and distance from it—unlike the intermediateness between the poles. For the sun's relation to them is equal, and the inhabitants of that middle are always at zenith or near it. The argument would only be valid if the extreme of heat and cold were under the two points of south and north, which is not the case. The equinox line (khaṭṭ al-istiwāʾ): it is an imaginary line starting from east to west under the orbit of the head of Aries (burj al-ḥamal). Night and day are always equal on that line, and the two poles there are always on the horizon, one towards the orbit of Canopus (Suhayl) in the south, and the other in the north towards the Goat (al-Jady). Here is an example of that: line, south pole, southern quarter, northern quarter, north pole, the equator.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an objection. The objector argues that if being central between climates indicates moderation for the fourth climate, then the equinox line is even more central as it lies on the boundary between north and south. The author answers by distinguishing two types of intermediateness. The fourth climate is intermediate between causes of heat and cold (zenith proximity and distance). The equinox line, by contrast, is always near zenith, so it is not truly intermediate between heat and cold causes. The author clarifies that the equinox line is an imaginary line where day and night are always equal and the poles are on the horizon. He concludes with a description and diagram of the line, including the positions of the poles and quadrants.

Key Terms
توسط (tawassuṭ, intermediateness)
مسامتة (musāmata, zenith)
قطب (quṭb, pole)
خط الاستواء (khaṭṭ al-istiwāʾ, equinox line)
فجاجة شمالية (fajāja shamāliyya, northern harshness)
احتراق جنوبي (iḥtirāq janūbī, southern burning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The equinox line (equator) is the most moderate region because it is the midpoint between the north and south poles.
Correction: The author explains that true moderation requires being between the causes of heat and cold (nearness to zenith and distance from it), not simply being geographically central. The equator's inhabitants are always at or near zenith, so they experience constant heat, not moderation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the argument that the equinox line is more moderate than the fourth climate?
A) Because the equator has longer days in summer.
B) Because the inhabitants of the equator are never at zenith.
C) Because the equator experiences constant near-zenith sun, not an intermediate between heat and cold causes.
D) Because the fourth climate has more buildings and population.

Key Takeaway
The moderation of a climate depends on its distance from the zenith and extremes of heat and cold, not on its geographic position between the poles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to seek balance in life, but are you simply choosing the geographic middle—the easy center—instead of the more difficult position between the real causes of spiritual growth? The equinox line stays near the zenith, never truly facing the extremes. Where in your life are you avoiding the northern harshness of discipline or the southern burning of sacrifice, settling for a comfortable 'moderation' that is actually just laziness?
?
The author says true moderation is being between the causes of heat and cold, not just between poles. What are the 'causes' in your own life that produce spiritual heat (zeal, effort, worship) and cold (apathy, ease, indifference)? Are you positioned between them, or are you clinging to a safe middle that never tests you?
?
Consider the sun's zenith: the equinox line is always under it, never knowing even the bearable distance of the fourth climate. Have you made comfort your constant companion, always staying in the light of what feels easy, avoiding the shadows of what requires sacrifice? Repent from this false moderation and ask Allah to place you where the real balancing tension—between hope and fear, effort and reliance—can shape your soul.

The Three Generated Beings: Minerals, Plants, Animals
Classification of composite bodies into three categories using two principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how composite bodies are classified into minerals, plants, and animals based on the presence of the principles of nutrition/growth and sensation/voluntary motion, and why the boundaries are not absolute.

Core Idea
Classification of composites hinges on two principles: nutrition/growth and sensation/motion.

In the study of composite bodies, theologians and philosophers have identified three fundamental categories: minerals, plants, and animals. This classification is not arbitrary but is based on observable principles. Sanusi explains the logical divisions of the 'composite' (al-mumtazij) and then raises an intriguing point about plants possibly possessing sensation, showing that natural categories can have blurred edges.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the complete Arabic text once, then read the translation. Notice the logical structure: after a brief introduction, the author presents the three categories and the reason for exclusive enumeration.
The passage begins with 'بعد الفراغ من مقدمة القسم الرابع' linking back to previous discussions.
2
Identify the two criteria used
Look for the two principles: 'مبدأ التغذية والتنمية' (principle of nutrition and growth) and 'مبدأ الحس والحركة الإرادية' (principle of sensation and voluntary motion). Understand how they are used to separate the three groups.
3
Understand the logical tree
Trace the decision tree: Does the composite have nutrition/growth? If no, it is mineral. If yes, does it have sensation/motion? If yes, animal; if no, plant.
This tree is the heart of the classification.
4
Note the qualification about plants
The author adds that we cannot be absolutely certain that plants lack sensation and will. He gives the example of the female date palm's 'yearning' for the male. This shows the author's awareness of empirical evidence that challenges rigid categories.
This softens the classification and reflects the cautious approach of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد الفراغ من مقدمة القسم الرابع من الأقسام الأربعة المرتب عليها الكلام في ما يتعلق بالأجسام على التفصيل وهو في المركبات التي لها مزاج شرع في مباحثه. وهي ثلاثة حسب حسب أقسام الممتزج المسماة بالمواليد الثلاثة. أعني المعادن والنبات والحيوان ووجه الحصر أن الممتزج إن تحقق فيه مبدأ التغذية والتنمية . فإما مع تحقق مبدأ الحس والحركة الإرادية، وهو الحيوان، أو بدونه وهو النبات، وإنه لم يتحقق ذلك فيه، فالمعادن. وإنما قلنا تحقق مبدأ الحس مع والحركة ، لأنه لا قطع بعدهما في النبات والمعدن . بل ربما يدعى حصول الشعور والإرادة للنبات، لامارات تدل على ذلك، مثل ما نشاهد من ميل النخلة الأنثى إلى الذكر وتعشقها ، به بحيث لو لم تلقح منه لم تثمر
After finishing the introduction of the fourth section among the four sections upon which the discourse is arranged concerning bodies in detail, and it is about composites that have a temperament, he began its investigations. And they are three, according to the divisions of the composite called the three generated beings: namely minerals, plants, and animals. And the reason for the exclusive enumeration is that if the principle of nutrition and growth is realized in the composite, then either with the realization of the principle of sensation and voluntary motion — and that is the animal — or without it — and that is the plant — and if it is not realized in it, then it is minerals. And we only said the realization of the principle of sensation with motion, because there is no certainty of their absence in plants and minerals. Rather, it is perhaps claimed that plants possess feeling and will, due to indications pointing to that, such as what we observe of the inclination of the female date palm towards the male and its yearning for it, such that if it is not pollinated by it, it does not bear fruit.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage classifies composite bodies (al-mumtazij) that have a temperament into three categories: minerals, plants, and animals. The classification is based on whether the principle of nutrition and growth (mabda' al-taghdhiya wa al-tanmiya) is present. If it is present, then either the principle of sensation and voluntary motion (mabda' al-hiss wa al-haraka al-iradiyya) is also present — that is the animal — or it is absent — that is the plant. If the principle of nutrition and growth is absent, the body is mineral. The author then qualifies his statement: he says 'we only said the realization of the principle of sensation with motion' because there is no absolute certainty (qat') that plants and minerals lack these. In fact, some claim that plants possess a kind of feeling (shu'ur) and will (irada) based on observable indications, such as the female date palm's inclination towards the male and its 'love' for it, to the extent that if it is not pollinated, it does not bear fruit. This shows that the boundaries between plant and animal are not rigid in the Aristotelian framework, and the author acknowledges the possibility of sensation in plants.

Key Terms
الممتزج
المواليد الثلاثة
مبدأ التغذية والتنمية
مبدأ الحس والحركة الإرادية
المعادن
النبات
الحيوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The classification of living things into plants and animals is absolute and plants definitely lack sensation.
Correction: The author states there is no certainty (qat') that plants lack sensation and will. He cites observations like the date palm's behavior as possible evidence of feeling.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes animals from plants?
A) The presence of nutrition and growth
B) The presence of sensation and voluntary motion
C) The presence of a temperament
D) The absence of minerals

Key Takeaway
Composite bodies are classified into minerals, plants, and animals based on the presence of nutrition/growth and sensation/voluntary motion, but the author notes that the absence of sensation in plants is not certain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are classified as an animal because you possess both nutrition/growth and sensation/voluntary motion. But how often do you live as if you were merely a plant—consuming and growing without conscious direction? Today, identify one area of your life where you can exercise your will more deliberately, and commit to doing so.
?
The author leaves open the possibility that plants may have feeling and will, citing the date palm's yearning. Have you ever dismissed the non-human creation as insentient? Will you now treat every living thing with greater reverence, knowing that their inner states may be beyond your certainty? What is one practical change this demands of you?
?
Your body is a composite with a temperament—a sign of deeper metaphysical principles. Are you living in a way that reflects your true nature as a creature with sensation and will, or are you sleepwalking through life? What would it mean for you to fully activate your humanity today?

The Three Kingdoms of Mixed Bodies
Classification of mixed bodies into three kingdoms with criteria and a defense of plant sensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam classifies mixed bodies into minerals, plants, and animals using the principles of nutrition/growth and sensation/motion.

Core Idea
Mixed bodies are judged by nutrition and sensation.

In Islamic cosmology, the material world is not a uniform blob. Theologians and philosophers classified compounds with a mixture (mumtazij) into three kingdoms based on whether they possess life functions. This classification reveals a graded view of creation, where even plants may have a faint trace of sensation.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the author's full argument. Notice how he first gives the classification and then defends the possibility of sensation in plants.
The passage is a single unit with a main statement followed by a philosophical justification.
2
Identify the three categories and criteria
Identify the three categories (minerals, plants, animals) and the two criteria: principle of nutrition/growth and principle of sensation/motion. See how animals have both, plants have only nutrition, minerals have neither.
3
Note the nuance about plants
Notice that the author does not categorically deny sensation in plants. He gives examples (palm tree, roots, branches) and explains that due to gradual gradation of mixtures, sensation may weaken but not vanish.
This shows the author's caution and philosophical depth.
4
Reflect on the philosophical principle
Understand why the author says this is not far from philosophical principles: mixtures vary gradually, so properties fade gradually. This connects to broader themes of emanation and hierarchy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ( وأما المباحث ) المبحث الأول ففي أقسام الممتزج وتسمي المواليد وهي المعدنيات والنبات والحيوان . لأنه إن تحقق فيها مبدأ التغذية والتنمية، فإما مع مبدأ الحس والحركة وهو الحيوان أو لا وهو النبات وإلا فالمعادن ولا قطع لعدم الحس والحركة فيهما، بل ربما يدعي ذلك في النبات ويستشهد بالامارات. ولما كان اختلاف مراتب الصورة في الكمال باختلاف الأمزجة في القرب من الاعتدال، لم يبعد أن لا ينتهي نقصان الاستحقاق في البعض إلى حد الانتفاء، بل الضعف والخفاء. بعد الفراغ من مقدمة القسم الرابع من الأقسام الأربعة المرتب عليها الكلام في فيما يتعلق بالأجسام على التفصيل وهو في المركبات التي لها مزاج شرع في مباحثه. وهي ثلاثة حسب أقسام الممتزج المسماة بالمواليد الثلاثة. أعني المعادن والنبات والحيوان ووجه الحصر أن الممتزج إن تحقق فيه مبدأ التغذية والتنمية . فإما مع تحقق مبدأ الحس والحركة الإرادية، وهو الحيوان، أو بدونه وهو النبات، وإن لم يتحقق ذلك فيه، فالمعادن. وإنما قلنا تحقق مبدأ الحس والحركة ، لأنه لا قطع بعدهما في النبات والمعدن . بل ربما يدعى حصول الشعور والإرادة للنبات، لامارات تدل على ذلك، مثل ما نشاهد من ميل النخلة الأنثى إلى الذكر وتعشقها ، به بحيث لو لم تلقح منه لم تثمر ، وميل عروق الأشجار إلى جهة الماء، وميل أغصانها في الصعود من جانب الموانع إلى الفضاء، ثم ليس هذا ببعيد عن القواعد الفلسفية فإن تباعد الأمزجة عن الاعتدال الحقيقي، إنما هو على غاية من التدريج، فانتقاص استحقاق الصور الحيوانية وخواصها، لا بد أن تبلغ قبل الانتفاء إلى حد الضعف والخفاء، وكذا النباتية .
He said: 'As for the investigations, the first investigation concerns the divisions of the mixed body and they are called the generated things: minerals, plants, and animals. For if the principle of nutrition and growth is realized in them, then either with the principle of sensation and motion—that is the animal—or not—that is the plant—otherwise they are minerals. There is no certainty that sensation and motion are absent in them; rather, that is sometimes claimed for plants and evidence is adduced. And since the degrees of form in perfection differ by the closeness of mixtures to equilibrium, it is not far-fetched that the deficiency of deserving in some does not reach the point of non-existence, but rather weakness and concealment.' After finishing the introduction of the fourth section of the four sections upon which the discourse is arranged concerning bodies in detail—that is, concerning compounds that have a mixture—he began its investigations. They are three according to the divisions of the mixed body called the three generated things: minerals, plants, and animals. The reason for the enumeration is that in the mixed body, if the principle of nutrition and growth is realized, then either with the realization of the principle of sensation and voluntary motion—that is the animal—or without it—that is the plant—and if it is not realized in it, then minerals. We only said 'realization of the principle of sensation and motion' because there is no certainty concerning their absence in plants and minerals. Rather, the possession of awareness and will is sometimes claimed for plants, due to indications that point to that, such as what we observe of the inclination of the female palm tree toward the male and its love for it, such that if it is not pollinated by it it does not bear fruit, and the inclination of tree roots toward water, and the inclination of their branches in rising from the side of obstacles toward open space. Then this is not far from philosophical principles, for the departure of mixtures from true equilibrium is only in extreme gradation; so the deficiency of the deservability of animal forms and their properties must, before non-existence, reach the stage of weakness and concealment, and likewise for plant forms.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the classification of mixed bodies (al-mumtazij) into three categories: minerals, plants, and animals. The author begins by stating that the first investigation concerns the divisions of the mixed body, which are called the 'generated things' (al-mawālīd): minerals, plants, and animals. The criterion for classification is the presence of the principle of nutrition and growth (mabda' al-taghdiya wa al-tanmiya). If this principle is present and accompanied by the principle of sensation and motion (mabda' al-ḥiss wa al-ḥaraka), it is an animal; if present without sensation and motion, it is a plant; if absent entirely, it is a mineral. The author notes that we are not absolutely certain that sensation and motion are absent in plants and minerals; indeed, some claim sensation for plants, citing examples like the palm tree's attraction to its opposite gender, the direction of roots toward water, and the upward growth of branches. This possibility is not far from philosophical principles because mixtures vary in their distance from true equilibrium in a gradual manner; thus, the entitlement to animal forms and their properties may weaken and become hidden before disappearing entirely. Similarly for plant forms. The commentary then elaborates on the structure of the discourse, noting that after the introduction to the fourth section, the author begins the detailed investigation of bodies, focusing on compounds with a mixture, and reiterates the three categories based on the mixed body.

Key Terms
الممتزج (al-mumtazij)
المواليد (al-mawālīd)
المعادن (al-ma'ādin)
النبات (al-nabāt)
الحيوان (al-ḥayawān)
مبدأ التغذية والتنمية (mabda' al-taghdiya wa al-tanmiya)
مبدأ الحس والحركة (mabda' al-ḥiss wa al-ḥaraka)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Plants are completely devoid of any sensation or awareness.
Correction: The text argues that while plants lack evident sensation and motion like animals, it is not certain they have no sensation at all. Evidence from plant behavior suggests they may have a weak, hidden form of awareness.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what distinguishes animals from plants in the classification of mixed bodies?
A) Animals have nutrition and growth, while plants do not.
B) Animals have sensation and motion, while plants have only nutrition and growth.
C) Animals are made of earth, plants of water.
D) Animals are created from minerals, plants from animals.

Key Takeaway
Mixed bodies are classified as minerals, plants, or animals based on whether they possess the principles of nutrition/growth and sensation/motion, with plants possibly having a faint degree of sensation due to gradual gradation of mixtures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Are you content with being 'weak and concealed' in your faith, or do you feel the urgency to grow toward greater equilibrium? Commit to one small step today to move closer to the 'true equilibrium' of your soul.
?

When you look at a rock or a tree, do you see it as merely inert matter, or as a sign of a continuous chain of being that includes you? Broad Definitions of Animal, Plant, and Mineral
The author broadens the three-kingdom definitions to achieve complete enumeration of genera.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author broadens the definitions of animal, plant, and mineral to include parts, derivatives, and artificial products, ensuring a complete enumeration of genera.

Core Idea
Animal, plant, and mineral definitions are stretched to cover everything in existence.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential gap in the three-kingdom classification: certain things like animal parts, honey, or manufactured goods might not obviously fit. By extending 'animal' to include its parts and derivatives, 'plant' to include fruits and derived substances, and 'mineral' to include all mixed bodies even if man-made, the enumeration of genera becomes exhaustive. This lesson examines that expansion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's own words establish the definitions.
2
Identify the three definitions and their expansions
Notice how each definition explicitly includes additional items: parts and derivatives for animal, derived substances for plant, artificial products for mineral.
3
Understand the purpose and the objection
The author states the purpose is complete enumeration of genera. Then he indicates this addresses an objection about things without sensation or motion but not plant/mineral.
The objection is repelled by the broad definitions.
4
Reflect on the impossibility of enumerating species
The author notes that enumerating species is humanly impossible, underscoring the sufficiency of genera enumeration.
This is a key limitation of human knowledge in classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ( والمراد بالحيوان ) ما يعمه وأجزاءه ومتولداته كالشعر والعظم واللبن والابريسم واللؤلؤ. وبالنبات ما يعم نحو الأشجار والأثمار، وما يتخذ منها، والمرجان وبالمعدن ما سوى ذلك من الممتزجات ولو بالصنعة كالزنجار والسنجرف، ليتم حصر الأجناس . وأما حصر الأنواع فلا سبيل إليه للبشر . إشارة إلى دفع ما يورد على حصر الأجناس في الثلاثة . حيث يوجد أشياء ليس فيها مبدأ الحس والحركة ، مع القطع بأنها ليست من النبات أو المعدن، كبعض أجزاء الحيوان ومتولداته كالعظم والشعر واللبن والعسل واللؤلؤ والابريسم، وما أشبه ذلك، وأشياء لا يطلق عليها اسم النبات والمعدن كالثمار وما يتخذ منها، وكالزنجار والسنجرف ونحو ذلك
He said: 'And what is meant by animal is what encompasses it, its parts, and its derivatives, such as hair, bone, milk, silk, and pearls. By plant, what encompasses trees, fruits, and what is taken from them, and coral. By mineral, everything else of the mixed bodies, even if by artifice, such as verdigris and red lead, so that the enumeration of the genera is complete. As for enumerating the species, there is no way for humans to do so.' This is an indication to repel what is objected concerning the enumeration of the genera into three, since there exist things that do not have the principle of sensation and motion, while being certain that they are not from plants or minerals, such as some parts of animals and their derivatives like bone, hair, milk, honey, pearls, and silk, and the like, and things that are not called by the name plant or mineral, such as fruits and what is taken from them, and like verdigris and red lead and the like.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage provides broad definitions for the three kingdoms—animal, plant, and mineral—to ensure that the enumeration of genera (jins, pl. ajnās) is complete and to address objections. The author states that 'animal' includes not only the animal itself but also its parts (e.g., hair, bone) and derivatives (e.g., milk, silk, pearls). 'Plant' includes trees, fruits, and derived substances like coral. 'Mineral' includes all other mixed bodies, even those produced by human artifice (e.g., verdigris, red lead). The purpose is to make the enumeration of genera exhaustive. However, the author adds that enumerating species (anwā') is impossible for humans. The commentary explains that this broad definition is a response to the objection that some things (like animal parts) lack sensation and motion yet are not plants or minerals, and similarly fruits and artificial products are not typically called plants or minerals. By defining animal and plant broadly to include their parts and derivatives, and mineral to include artificial mixed bodies, the classification covers all such items.

Definitions of the Three Kingdoms and Defense of Their Limitation
Definitions of animal, plant, and mineral with refutation of an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of animal, plant, and mineral according to this author, and how he defends the limitation to three genera against the objection of intermediate products.

Core Idea
The three kingdoms are defined broadly to include their parts and products, making the enumeration complete.

The author begins by establishing precise definitions for the three genera—animal, plant, and mineral—not just as living beings but including their derived products. He then addresses a potential objection: there exist things that are clearly not plants or minerals yet lack sensation. His response shows that these are already covered under the definition of animal, thus preserving the threefold classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Notice how each genus is defined to include its parts and products, not just the living being itself. For example, animal includes hair, bone, milk, silk, and pearls.
2
Identify the objection
What things seem to fall outside the three categories? The author mentions animal parts and products that lack sensation, and plant products like fruits and artificial mixtures like verdigris.
3
Understand the author's response
How does he show these are still within the three? By noting that the definitions already encompass these items as parts, products, or mixtures.
4
Reflect on the completeness
Why is enumerating species impossible for humans? The author states that species enumeration is beyond human capacity, but genera enumeration is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا اتفقوا على أن من المعدنيات ما وصل إلى أفق النباتية، ومن النباتات ما وصل إلى أفق الحيوانية كالنخلة، وإليه الإشارة بقوله عليه السلام «أكرموا عمتكم النخلة». قال (والمراد بالحيوان) ما يعمه وأجزاءه ومتولداته كالشعر والعظم واللبن والابريسم واللؤلؤ. وبالنبات ما يعم نحو الأشجار والأثمار، وما يتخذ منها، والمرجان وبالمعدن ما سوى ذلك من الممتزجات ولو بالصنعة كالزنجار والسنجرف، ليتم حصر الأجناس. وأما حصر الأنواع فلا سبيل إليه للبشر. إشارة إلى دفع ما يورد على حصر الأجناس في الثلاثة حيث يوجد أشياء ليس فيها مبدأ الحس والحركة، مع القطع بأنها ليست من النبات أو المعدن، كبعض أجزاء الحيوان ومتولداته كالعظم والشعر واللبن والعسل واللؤلؤ والابريسم، وما أشبه ذلك، وأشياء لا يطلق عليها اسم النبات والمعدن كالثمار وما يتخذ منها، وكالزنجار والسنجرف ونحو ذلك.
Therefore, they agreed that among minerals there is what reaches the horizon of plants, and among plants there is what reaches the horizon of animals, like the palm tree, and to this points his saying, peace be upon him: 'Honor your aunt the palm tree.' He said: (And by 'animal' is meant) that which encompasses it and its parts and its products, such as hair, bone, milk, silk, and pearls. And by 'plant' that which encompasses trees, fruits, and what is taken from them, and coral, and by 'mineral' everything else of mixtures, even if by artifice, like verdigris and vermilion, so that the enumeration of the genera is complete. As for the enumeration of species, there is no way for humans to do it. A reference to repelling what is objected to regarding confining the genera to three, where there exist things that have no principle of sensation and movement, while it is certain that they are not from plants or minerals, such as some parts of animals and their products like bone, hair, milk, honey, pearls, silk, and the like; and things that are not called plant or mineral, like fruits and what is taken from them, and like verdigris, vermilion, and the like.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage begins by affirming the gradual continuity between the three kingdoms: minerals can reach the horizon of plants, and plants can reach the horizon of animals, exemplified by the palm tree, and cites a hadith. It then provides precise definitions: 'animal' includes the body, its parts, and its products; 'plant' includes trees, fruits, and derived items like coral; 'mineral' is everything else, including artificially produced mixtures like verdigris. The purpose is to achieve a complete enumeration of genera (أجناس). The author then notes that enumerating species is beyond human capacity. Finally, he addresses an objection: there are items that lack sensation and movement yet are clearly not plant or mineral, such as animal parts (bone, hair) and products (milk, silk). The response is implicit: these are included in the definition of 'animal' as its parts and products, so the three genera suffice.

Classification of Minerals and Definition of Fusible Metals
Classification and definition of minerals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's classification of minerals into five types and the precise definition of the first type, fusible and malleable metals, including the concepts of malleability (الانطراق) and melting (الذوبان).

Core Idea
Minerals are classified by fusibility and malleability; metals are defined by a unique congealed state.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a systematic classification of minerals based on their behavior under fire. The author first gives a binary division, then a fivefold one, and finally defines the first category—fusible and malleable metals—in detail. This lesson will unpack that classification and the key concepts of malleability and melting.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the overall structure.
Notice the author begins a new section (المبحث الأول).
2
Identify the two classifications
The author first gives a binary classification (fusible vs non-fusible) and then a fivefold division. Locate both in the text.
The fivefold division is clearly numbered in the Arabic.
3
Analyze the definition of the first type
Focus on the definition of 'ذائب منطرق' (fusible and malleable). Note the conditions: congealed moist and dry, inseparable by fire, strong greasiness, and the description of malleability and melting.
Malleability is described as 'thrusting into depth with extension in length and width little by little, without separation'.
4
Review the list of seven metals
The passage lists the seven well-known fusible and malleable metals. Notice the mention of zinc and its special use for mirrors.
The list: gold, silver, lead, tin, iron, copper, zinc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول. المعدني إما ذائب مع الانطراق أو الاشتعال أو بدونهما، وإما غير ذائب لفرط رطوبته أو يبوسته. فالأول كالأجساد السبعة الذهب والفضة والرصاص والأسرب والحديد وإذابته بالحيلة، والنحاس والخارصيني وتكونها من الزئبق والكبريت على حسب اختلاف صفاتهما وامتزاجاتهما بشهادة الأمارات، وعدم وجدانهما في معادنهما يجوز أن يكون لتغيرهما أو بصغر جرمهما جداً. أقسام المعدني خمسة: ذائب منطرق. ذائب مشتعل. ذائب غير منطوق ولا مشتعل. غير ذائب لفرط الرطوبة. غير ذائب لفرط اليبوسة. فالأول: أي الذائب المنطرق هو الجسم الذي انجمد فيه الرطب واليابس بحيث لا تقدر النار على تفريقهما مع بقاء دهنية قوية بسببها يقبل ذلك الجسم الانطراق وهو الاندفاع في العمق بانبساط يعرض للجسم في الطول والعرض قليلاً قليلاً، دون انفصال شيء، والذوبان سيلان الجسم بسبب تلازم رطبه ويابسه. والمشهور من أنواع الذائب المنطرق سبعة: الذهب والفضة والرصاص والأسرب والحديد والنحاس والخارصيني، قيل هو جوهر شبيه بالنحاس يتخذ منه مراي لها خواص.
The first research. The mineral is either fusible with malleability or combustibility or without them, or non-fusible due to excessive moisture or dryness. The first type is like the seven bodies: gold, silver, lead, tin, iron and its melting by artifice, copper, and zinc; and their formation from mercury and sulfur according to the difference of their qualities and mixtures, as evidenced by signs, and their not being found in their mines may be due to their change or their very small size. The divisions of the mineral are five: fusible and malleable; fusible and combustible; fusible without being malleable or combustible; non-fusible due to excessive moisture; non-fusible due to excessive dryness. The first: i.e., the fusible and malleable is the body in which the moist and dry have solidified such that fire cannot separate them, with a strong greasiness remaining, due to which that body accepts malleability, which is a thrusting into depth with an extension occurring to the body in length and width little by little, without separation of anything. And melting is the flowing of the body due to the conjunction of its moist and dry. The well-known types of fusible and malleable are seven: gold, silver, lead, tin, iron, copper, and zinc. It is said that it is a substance similar to copper from which mirrors with special properties are made.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage begins a new section (المبحث الأول) on minerals. The author first presents a binary classification: minerals are either fusible (with malleability or combustion or without) or non-fusible (due to excess moisture or dryness). The first category is exemplified by the seven metals, and he notes their formation from mercury and sulfur. He then provides a fivefold division of minerals: 1) fusible and malleable, 2) fusible and combustible, 3) fusible but neither malleable nor combustible, 4) non-fusible due to excess moisture, 5) non-fusible due to excess dryness. He then defines the first type in detail: the fusible and malleable body is one where moist and dry are congealed so that fire cannot separate them, retaining a strong greasiness, allowing it to be hammered and extended without breaking. Melting is the flowing due to cohesion of moist and dry. Finally, he lists the seven well-known fusible and malleable metals: gold, silver, lead, tin, iron, copper, and zinc, with a note that zinc is a substance like copper used for mirrors.

Key Takeaway
Minerals are classified into five types, with the fusible and malleable defined by the congealed state of moist and dry that fire cannot separate, allowing hammering without breaking.

The Five Types of Minerals
Classification of minerals into five types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how minerals are classified into five categories based on whether they are molten or not, and the subcategories within each.

Core Idea
Minerals are classified by fusibility and moisture into five distinct types.

This short passage presents a foundational taxonomy of minerals. The author first divides minerals into molten and non-molten, then further subdivides each category. Understanding this classification is essential for the subsequent discussion of metals and their properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the two main divisions and the five specific types listed at the end.
The passage is very concise; every word counts.
2
Identify the two main categories
The first sentence divides minerals into 'molten' (ذائب) and 'non-molten' (غير ذائب). Note the characteristics that define non-molten: excess moisture or excess dryness.
3
List the five types
From the enumeration, list each of the five types in order. Compare them to the earlier description to see how they map onto the two categories.
The author explicitly says 'أقسام المعدني خمسة' (the divisions of minerals are five).
4
Understand the criteria
For molten minerals, the criteria are malleability (انطراق) and combustibility (اشتعال). For non-molten, it is excess moisture or dryness. Ask yourself why these criteria matter for classification.
This classification likely links to practical or philosophical uses.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why a scholar would create this taxonomy. It helps organize knowledge about minerals, similar to how biological species are classified.
This is a typical approach in traditional sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المعدني إما ذائب مع الانطراق أو الاشتعال أو بدونهما، وإما غير ذائب لفرط رطوبته أو يبوسته. أقسام المعدني خمسة: ذائب منطرق. ذائب مشتعل. ذائب غير منطوق ولا مشتعل. غير ذائب لفرط الرطوبة. غير ذائب لفرط اليبوسة.
Minerals are either molten with malleability, or combustion, or without them, or not molten due to excess of its moisture or its dryness. The divisions of minerals are five: molten and malleable; molten and combustible; molten, neither malleable nor combustible; not molten due to excess moisture; not molten due to excess dryness.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of minerals. It begins by dividing minerals into two main categories: those that are molten (ذائب) and those that are not molten (غير ذائب). The molten category has three subcategories: (1) molten with malleability (انطراق), (2) molten with combustion (اشتعال), and (3) molten with neither. The non-molten category has two subcategories: (1) non-molten due to excess moisture (فرط رطوبته), and (2) non-molten due to excess dryness (فرط يبوسته). The author then enumerates the five types explicitly: ذائب منطرق (molten malleable), ذائب مشتعل (molten combustible), ذائب غير منطوق ولا مشتعل (molten neither malleable nor combustible), غير ذائب لفرط الرطوبة (not molten due to excess moisture), and غير ذائب لفرط اليبوسة (not molten due to excess dryness). This classification is a foundational taxonomy for the subsequent discussion of metals.

Definition of the First Mineral Type: The Molten Malleable
Defines the first mineral type and its properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the defining characteristics of the first type of mineral, the molten malleable, including the concepts of solidification, greasiness, malleability, and melting.

Core Idea
Malleable minerals resist fire separation due to a cohesive greasy moisture.

In this passage, the author begins by defining the first mineral type, known as the molten malleable (الذائب المنطرق). He explains that such bodies have a unique balance of moisture and dryness that fire cannot break apart, and they retain a strong greasiness enabling malleability. Understanding this definition is foundational for classifying minerals in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the opening sentence carefully. Note the terms for body, moist, dry, and fire.
2
Understand malleability
The author describes malleability as gradual spreading without separation. Reflect on how this differs from other physical changes.
3
Clarify melting
Melting is defined as flowing from cohesion. Compare it with malleability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالأول : أي الذائب المنطرق هو الجسم الذي انجمد فيه الرطب واليابس بحيث لا تقدر النار على تفريقهما مع بقاء دهنية قوية بسببها يقبل ذلك الجسم الانطراق وهو الاندفاع في العمق بانبساط يعرض للجسم في الطول والعرض قليلاً قليلاً، دون انفصال شيء، والذوبان سيلان الجسم بسبب تلازم رطبه ويابسه والمشهور من أنواع الذائب المنطرق سبعة : الذهب والفضة والرصاص والأسرب والحديد والنحاس والخارصيني، قيل هو جوهر شبيه بالنحاس يتخذ منه مراي لها خواص. وذكر الخازني أنه لا يوجد في عهدنا والذي يتخذ منه مرايا ويسمى بالحديد الصيني، والانقنجوسن فجوهر مركب من بعض الفلزات وليس بالخارصيني والذوبان في غير الحديد ظاهر وأما في الحديد فيكون بالحيلة على ما يعرفه أرباب الصنعة.
Thus the first, i.e., the molten malleable, is the body in which the moist and the dry have solidified such that fire cannot separate them while a strong greasiness remains, by virtue of which that body accepts malleability, which is the pushing in depth by a spreading that occurs to the body in length and width little by little, without separation of anything. And melting is the flowing of the body due to the coherence of its moist and dry. And the well-known of the types of molten malleable are seven: gold, silver, lead, tin, iron, copper, and zinc. It is said it is a substance resembling copper from which mirrors with properties are made. Al-Khazini mentioned that it does not exist in our time and that from which mirrors are made and is called Chinese iron, and al-anqanjusan is a substance composed of some metals and is not zinc. And melting is apparent in other than iron, but in iron it is by artifice as known by the people of the craft.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of mineral, the molten malleable (الذائب المنطرق). The author explains that such a body has a balance of moisture (رطب) and dryness (يابس) solidified together so that fire cannot separate them, and it retains a strong greasiness (دهنية) that allows it to be malleable (انطراق). Malleability is described as a gradual spreading in length and width without separation. Melting (الذوبان) is the flowing of the body due to the cohesion of its moist and dry parts. Then the author lists the seven well-known types of this category: gold, silver, lead, tin, iron, copper, and zinc (الخارصيني). He adds notes about zinc: it resembles copper and is used for mirrors; al-Khazini reports that it is no longer found in their time and that the material called 'Chinese iron' is used for mirrors, and that 'al-anqanjusan' is a compound not identical to zinc. Finally, he remarks that melting is obvious for metals other than iron, but for iron it requires artifice (الحيلة) as known by craftsmen.

Key Takeaway
The first mineral type is defined by a solidified mixture of moist and dry that resists fire and retains greasiness for malleability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Seven Malleable Metals and Special Notes
Enumerates the seven known malleable metals and adds clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the seven well-known malleable metals and understand the clarifications regarding zinc and the melting of iron.

Core Idea
Seven metals are listed, with special notes distinguishing zinc from a composite and iron needing artifice to melt.

After defining the first mineral type, the author lists the seven well-known metals that belong to this category. He then provides important clarifications about one of them, zinc (الخارصيني), and notes that iron requires special treatment to melt. These details help identify the metals accurately and understand their properties.

Guided Path
1
List the seven metals
Read the list of seven metals: gold, silver, lead, tin, iron, copper, and zinc. Memorize them.
2
Note the clarification about zinc
The author mentions that zinc resembles copper and is used for mirrors, but it may be confused with a compound called al-anqanjusan.
3
Understand the melting of iron
Iron does not melt easily; it requires artifice according to craftsmen.

The Core Theory: Metals Are Made of Mercury and Sulfur
Sanusi states the mercury-sulfur theory, provides empirical signs, and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central claim that all seven metals originate from mercury and sulfur, the observable signs supporting it, and the response to a key objection.

Core Idea
Mercury and sulfur are the primal materials of all metals, proven by signs and craft.

Sanusi introduces a foundational idea from natural philosophy: all seven metals share a common origin in mercury and sulfur. He presents observable signs that support this claim and then defends it against an objection from Abu al-Barakat. This lesson unpacks that evidence and the counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: claim, signs, objection, response.
The passage begins after a brief mention of Chinese iron.
2
Identify the main claim and the signs
Find where Sanusi states that the matter of the seven bodies is mercury and sulfur. Then list the three signs he mentions: lead melting, mercury coagulating, and mercury's composition.
The signs are called 'amarat' – indicators, not certain proofs.
3
Examine the objection and its refutation
Abu al-Barakat objects that if the theory were true, mercury and sulfur would be found in each other's mines. Sanusi offers two possible reasons for their absence. Understand why this refutation is considered valid by the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالحديد الصيني، والانقنجوسن فجوهر مركب من بعض الفلزات وليس بالخارصيني والذوبان في غير الحديد ظاهر وأما في الحديد فيكون بالحيلة على ما يعرفه أرباب الصنعة وشهدت الأمارات بأن مادة الأجسام السبعة الزئبق والكبريت، واختلاف الأنواع والأصناف عائد إلى اختلاف صفاتهما واختلاطهما، وتأثر أحدهما عن الآخر، أما الأمارات فهي أنها سيما الرصاص تذوب إلى مثل الزئبق، والزئبق ينعقد برائحة الكبريت إلى مثل الرصاص والزئبق يتعلق بهذه الأجساد، ثم الزئبق مركب من مائية وكبريتية. وامتحان علم الصنعة أيضاً يشهد بذلك . واعتراض أبي البركات بأنه لو كان الأمر كذلك لوجد كل من الزئبق والكبريت في معدن الآخر، وفي معادن هذه الأجسام مدفوع بأنه يجوز أن يكون عدم الوجدان لتغيرهما بالامتزاج ، أو لعدم الاحساس بواسطة تصغر الأجزاء، وأما كيفية تكونها، فهي أنه إذا كان الزئبق والكبريت صافيين، وكان انطباخ أحدهما بالآخر تاماً ، فإذا كان الكبريت مع بقائه أبيض غير محترق تكونت الفضة ، وإن كان أحمر وفيه قوة صباغة لطيفة غير محرقة تكون الذهب وإن كانا نقيين وفي الكبريت قوة صباغة لكن وصل إليه قبل كمال النضج برد مجمد عاقد يكون الخارصيني، وإن كان الزئبق نقياً، والكبريت رديئاً، فإن كان مع الرداءة فيه قوة إحراقية، تكون النحاس، وإن كان غير شديد المخالطة بالزئبق ، بل متداخلاً إياه سافاً فسافاً تولد الرصاص، وإن كان الزئبق والكبريت رديئين، فإن قوي التركيب، وفي الزئبق تخلخل أرضي وفي الكبريت إحراق تكون الحديد وإن ضعف التركيب تكون الأسرب، وأصحاب الصنعة يصححون هذه الدعاوي بعقد الزئبق بالكبريت انعقاداً محسوساً يحصل لهم بذلك غلبة الظن بأن الأحوال الطبيعية تقارب الأحوال الصناعية، وأما القطع فلا يد فيه لأحد .
For Chinese iron and al-anqanjus [?], it is a substance composed of some metals and not zinc. Melting in other than iron is apparent, but in iron it is by artifice as known by the practitioners of the craft. The signs testified that the matter of the seven bodies is mercury and sulfur, and the difference of types and species returns to the difference of their attributes and their mixture, and the influence of one upon the other. As for the signs, they are that especially lead melts into something like mercury, and mercury coagulates with the smell of sulfur into something like lead, and mercury adheres to these bodies. Then mercury is composed of an aqueous and a sulfurous part. The test of the science of the craft also bears witness to that. The objection of Abu al-Barakat that if it were so, then each of mercury and sulfur would be found in the mine of the other, and in the mines of these bodies, is repelled by the possibility that the non-finding is due to their change by mixing, or due to lack of sensation because of the smallness of parts. As for the manner of their formation, it is that if mercury and sulfur are pure and the cooking of one with the other is complete, then if the sulfur remains white and unburned, silver is formed. If it is red and has a fine coloring power that is not burning, gold is formed. If both are pure and in the sulfur there is a coloring power, but it reached it before complete maturity by a freezing, congealing cold, zinc is formed. If the mercury is pure and the sulfur is inferior, then if with inferiority it has a burning power, copper is formed. If it is not strongly mixed with mercury, but interpenetrates it layer by layer, lead is generated. If mercury and sulfur are both inferior, then if the composition is strong, and in the mercury there is earthly looseness and in the sulfur there is burning, iron is formed. If the composition is weak, lead (asrib) is formed. The practitioners of the craft verify these claims by the coagulation of mercury with sulfur in a perceptible coagulation, by which they obtain a predominant opinion that natural states approximate artificial states. As for certainty, no one has a hand in it.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage presents and defends the alchemical theory that all seven metals are composed of mercury and sulfur. The author first states the theory: the matter of the seven bodies (metals) is mercury and sulfur, and differences in species arise from differences in their attributes and mixture. He then provides observable signs (amarat) as evidence: lead melts into a substance resembling mercury, and mercury coagulates with sulfur into a substance resembling lead. He also notes that the test of the craft confirms this. Next, he addresses an objection from Abu al-Barakat, who argued that if this theory were true, mercury and sulfur should be found in each other's mines. The author replies that their absence could be due to transformation through mixture or because their particles are too small to sense. The author then explains how different metals are formed based on the purity of mercury and sulfur, the degree of cooking, and presence of impurities. For example, pure white sulfur yields silver; red sulfur with fine coloring yields gold; impure or mixed conditions produce zinc, copper, lead, iron, or inferior lead. Finally, he notes that practitioners achieve perceptible coagulation of mercury with sulfur, leading to strong probability that natural processes mirror artificial ones, but absolute certainty (qat‘) is unattainable. The passage thus combines empirical observation, theoretical reasoning, and acknowledgment of epistemic limits.

Key Terms
زئبق
كبريت
أمارات
الأجسام السبعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the alchemical theory was considered certain by its proponents.
Correction: Sanusi here only presents signs (amarat) and refutes an objection; he later states that absolute certainty is not attainable.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is presented as a sign that metals are composed of mercury and sulfur?
A) Lead melts into something resembling mercury.
B) Iron is found in the same mines as mercury.
C) All metals are white in their pure form.
D) The Quran mentions mercury and sulfur.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi defends the mercury-sulfur theory of metals using observable signs and a reasoned refutation of a key objection, without claiming absolute certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you base your beliefs on 'signs' rather than certainty, do you honestly admit that you could be wrong? When was the last time you changed a conviction because new evidence emerged?
?
Sanusi offered two possible explanations for why mercury and sulfur are not found together. In your own life, do you cling to comfortable explanations when the evidence seems to contradict your views? Are you willing to accept that you may lack full perception?
?
If you had to defend a fragile theory—like the alchemists did—would you be as transparent about its weaknesses as Sanusi was by including the objection? Or do you hide doubts to appear certain?
