Classification of Third and Fourth Digestion Residues
Detailed classification of residues from the third and fourth digestions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed classification of residues from the later digestions into natural/unnatural expulsion and further subcategories based on transformation and benefits.

Core Idea
Residues are either expelled naturally or unnaturally, and natural expulsion may combine reduction with another benefit.

Taftazani now delves into a complex classification of residues from the third and fourth digestions. He first distinguishes whether the expulsion is natural or unnatural. Unnatural residues can remain untransformed, be incompletely transformed, or completely transformed (suitable or not for nutrition). Natural expulsion is further divided based on whether it combines the benefit of reduction with another benefit, such as generating nails, hair, semen, or other fluids, or not.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification overview
Start at 'وأما الهضم الثالث والرابع' and read until the end. Identify the first major division: natural vs unnatural expulsion.
Unnatural residues are those not normally exiting the body.
2
Explore unnatural residues
Under unnatural, there are three types: remaining as untransformed humor, incompletely transformed (pus), and completely transformed (either suitable for nutrition or not).
Examples: hemorrhoid blood, pus, suet, tumor pus.
3
Analyze natural residues without additional benefit
Under natural expulsion, some residues only provide reduction (no extra benefit). These may form separate bodies (lice), non-separate (gravel), or no body (vapor, dirt).
Notice the examples: vapor, body dirt.
4
Understand natural residues with additional benefit
Some residues combine reduction with another benefit: generating nails, hair, semen, or aiding them (like wady, menses, milk), or repelling harm (earwax, saliva).
Saliva aids speech; earwax kills insects.

Source Text

أن يكون خروجاً طبيعياً أو لا، والثاني إما أن يكون باقياً على خلطيته من غير تصرف للهضم الثالث كدم البواسير، والدم الفاسد الخارج بالرعاف وغيره، وإما أن يكون قد استحال استحالة غير تامة كالصديد والقيح، أو تامة: إما إلى حالة تصلح للتغذية، كالثغل النضج الخارج من البول في حالة الصحة، مما فات الصفرة الغاذية، أو لا كالمدة الخارجة من الأورام المتفجرة، والأول وهو ما يكون خروجه طبيعياً. إما أن يجمع إلى منفعة الانتقاص منفعة أخرى أولا. فالأول إما أن يكون تلك المنفعة توليد جسم متصل بالبدن من جنس الأعضاء، وهو مادة الظفر أو لا وهو مادة الشعر، أو غير متصل، وهو مادة الولد. أعني المني، أو يكون غير توليد جسم آخر. وحينئذ فتلك المنفعة قد تتعلق بالمني، كالودي الرطوبة المني المسهل لخروجه، وقد يتعلق بالجنين حال تكونه كالطمث، أو حال خروجه كالرطوبات الكائنة حالة الولادة، أو بعد ذلك كاللبن، وقد لا تتعلق بهما، وذلك إما لدفع ضرر شيء يخرج من البدن كالودي الكاسر بلعابيته لحدة البول، أو يدخل فيه كوسخ الأذن القاتل بمرارته لما يدخل فيها من الذباب ونحوه، وإما لا لدفع ضرر شيء كاللعاب المعين على الكلام بترطيبه اللسان. والثاني. وهو ما لا يجمع إلى منفعة الانتقاص منفعة أخرى إما أن يتكون عنه جسم آخر منفصل كمادة القمل، أو غير منفصل كمادة الحصا، وإما أن لا يتكون، وهو إما أن لا يكون محسوساً البتة، كالبخار المتحلل، أو يكون محسوساً أحياناً كوسخ البدن الكائن من فضل غذائه، فإنه لا يحس إلا أن يجمع أو دائماً، وخروجه إما أن يكون من منفذ محسوس كالمخاط أو غير محسوس كالعرق. [قال فتصير الأخلاط الأربعة]
A misconception occurs to some minds that the first digestion is in the mouth, the second in the stomach, the third in the liver, and the fourth in the veins – error regarding what is the mainstay and goal in digestion, I mean the change into the substance of this organ being beyond consideration. As for making the hepatic digestion one, while its beginning is in the mesentery, and all — I mean the fine, hard veins connecting the liver and the ends of the stomach and intestines — and it has no union with the liver, because there is no parallel in them for the fine chyle drawn to them, a change to be reckoned with and a state distinct from the chylous state that occurred in the stomach and the humoral state that occurs in the liver. Then for each of these digestions there is a residue that is expelled, due to the necessity that the digestive power cannot transform all the food that comes to it, either because of its abundance, or because among its parts there are some that are not suitable to become part of the nourished one. So the first digestion has much residue, because it acts on the food while it remains in its natural state and its suitable and unsuitable parts, and upon its abundance arriving at the stomach, by the animal's choice, especially humans who, due to the balance of their temperament, need to diversify foods and increase them by combination and other means, not merely by natural attraction of the beneficial alone, as in the remaining digestions, and as in plant nutrition. Therefore it needed an outlet that can accommodate the abundance of wastes, which is the anus. The second digestion has little, fine residue, because it receives food by natural attraction and through very narrow passages, so most of it is excreted through urine, and the remainder via the spleen and gallbladder. As for the third and fourth digestions, the expulsion of their residues is either natural or not. The second is either remaining in its humoral state without transformation for the third digestion, like hemorrhoid blood and corrupt blood exiting through nosebleed and other means, or it may have undergone incomplete transformation like pus and matter, or complete transformation: either into a state suitable for nutrition, like the ripe suet excreted in urine in a state of health, which has missed the nourishing yellowness, or not, like the pus exiting from bursting tumors. The first – that is, what is naturally expelled – either combines with the benefit of reduction another benefit or not. The first: either that benefit is the generation of a body connected to the body of the same genus as the organs, which is the substance of the nail, or not, which is the substance of hair; or disconnected, which is the substance of the child, I mean semen; or it is not the generation of another body. Then that benefit may relate to the semen, like the wady, the moisture of semen that eases its exit; or it may relate to the fetus during its formation, like menses, or during its exit, like the moistures occurring during birth, or after that, like milk; or it may not relate to either of them, and that is either to repel harm of something exiting the body, like the wady that breaks the sharpness of urine by its saliva-like quality, or something entering it, like earwax, which kills by its bitterness what enters it from flies and the like; or not to repel harm, like saliva, which aids speech by moistening the tongue. The second – that is, what does not combine with the benefit of reduction another benefit – either results in another separate body, like the substance of lice, or non-separate, like the substance of gravel; or does not result in a body, and that is either completely imperceptible, like vapor that dissolves, or sometimes perceptible, like body dirt from the residue of its nutrition, for it is not sensed unless it accumulates or constantly. Its exit is either from a perceptible outlet, like mucus, or imperceptible, like sweat. [He said: Then the four humors are formed.]
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This is a classification and explanatory passage about the four digestions and the fate of their residues. The author begins by correcting a common misconception: some think the first digestion is in the mouth, second in stomach, third in liver, fourth in veins. He dismisses this as an error, explaining that the true focus is the transformation of food into the substance of the organ. He then argues that hepatic digestion should not be considered a single process because it actually begins in the mesentery (fine veins connecting liver to stomach and intestines) and there is no union with the liver that would cause a significant change in the chyle. Next, he states that each of these digestions produces a residue that must be expelled because the digestive power cannot transform all food due to abundance or unsuitable parts. He describes the first digestion's residue as large because the animal, especially humans, intentionally consumes varied and abundant food, requiring a large outlet (the anus). The second digestion's residue is fine and little because it receives food by natural attraction through narrow passages, so most is excreted via urine and the rest via spleen and gallbladder. The author then discusses the third and fourth digestions, categorizing the expulsion of their residues as either natural or unnatural. Unnatural expulsion includes matter that remains untransformed (e.g., hemorrhoid blood) or transformed incompletely (pus) or completely (either suitable for nutrition like suet, or not like tumor pus). Natural expulsion is further divided: either it combines the benefit of reduction with another benefit or not. If it combines, that benefit may be generation of a connected body (nail or hair), a disconnected body (semen), or not generation (e.g., fluids aiding semen, fetus, or repelling harm). If it does not combine, it may result in a separate body (lice) or non-separate (gravel), or no body (imperceptible vapor or perceptible dirt), with exit via perceptible or imperceptible outlets. The passage concludes with a transitional phrase indicating that from these residues the four humors are formed.

منفعة الانتقاص

الشعر

المخاط
البخار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All residues are simply waste with no further benefit.
Correction: Many natural residues serve additional functions like generating nails, hair, or aiding reproduction.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a residue that is expelled naturally but without any additional benefit?
A) Nail growth
B) Earwax
C) Body dirt (وسخ البدن)
D) Semen

Key Takeaway
The classification of residues demonstrates the sophisticated and functional view of the body in Islamic medical tradition, where waste often serves a purpose.

The Third Digestion and Transformation of Bodily Fluids
Introduces third digestion and transformation types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of the third digestion, the two types of transformation (complete and incomplete), and the initial classification of fluids that exit naturally.

Core Idea
Bodily fluids are not just waste; they undergo transformation and often serve purposes beyond reduction.

This passage opens with a discussion of the third digestion, a key concept in Islamic medicine that deals with the final stage of nutrient processing. The author categorizes fluids based on whether they have undergone incomplete or complete transformation, and then focuses on those that exit naturally, setting the stage for a detailed classification of their benefits.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the technical terms like 'هضم ثالث' and 'استحالة'.
2
Identify the two types of transformation
Find where the author distinguishes between incomplete transformation (pus) and complete transformation (nutritious or non-nutritious).
3
Understand 'natural exit' and the initial dichotomy
The passage then says 'The first, which is its natural exit, either combines with the benefit of reduction another benefit or not.' This is the branching point for the rest of the classification.
This sets up the detailed classification in the next lesson.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters: it shows that even waste products can have beneficial purposes, aligning with a purposeful view of creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصرف للهضم الثالث كدم البواسير، والدم الفاسد الخارج بالرعاف وغيره، وإما أن يكون قد استحال استحالة غير تامة كالصديد والقيح، أو تامة : إما إلى حالة تصلح للتغذية، كالثغل النضج الخارج من البول في حالة الصحة، مما فات الصفرة الغاذية ،أو ، ،لا كالمدة الخارجة من الأورام المتفجرة، والأول وهو ما يكون خروجه طبيعياً. إما أن يجمع إلى منفعة الانتقاص منفعة أخرى أولا . فالأول إما أن يكون تلك المنفعة توليد جسم متصل بالبدن من جنس الأعضاء، وهو مادة الظفر أو لا وهو مادة الشعر، أو غير متصل، وهو مادة .الولد. أعني المني، أو يكون غیر تولید جسم آخر. وحينئذ فتلك المنفعة قد تتعلق بالمني، كالـودي الرطوبة المني المسهل لخروجه، وقد يتعلق بالجنين حال تكونه كالطمث، أو حال خروجه كالرطوبات الكائنة حالة الولادة، أو بعد ذلك كاللبن، وقد لا تتعلق بهما، وذلك إما لدفع ضرر شيء يخرج من البدن كالودي الكاسر بلعابيته لحدة البول، أو يدخل فيه كوسخ الأذن القاتل بمرارته لما يدخل فيها من الذباب ونحوه، وإما لا لدفع ضرر شيء كاللعاب المعين على الكلام بترطيبه اللسان . والثاني. وهو ما لا يجمع إلى منفعة الانتقاص منفعة أخرى إما أن يتكون عنه جسم آخر منفصل كمادة القمل ، أو غير منفصل كمادة الحصا، وإما أن لا يتكون ، وهو إما أن لا يكون محسوساً ،البتة، كالبخار المتحلل ، أو يكون محسوساً أحياناً كوسخ البدن الكائن من فضل غذائه ، فإنه لا يحس إلا أن يجمع أو دائماً، وخروجه إما أن يكون من منفذ محسوس كالمخاط أو غير محسوس كالعرق.
Regarding the third digestion, like hemorrhoid blood, and the spoiled blood exiting through nosebleed and other than it; or it may have undergone incomplete transformation like pus and purulent discharge, or complete transformation: either to a state suitable for nutrition, like the ripe [؟] exiting from urine in a state of health, missing the nutritive yellowness, or not, like the discharge exiting from bursting abscesses. The first, which is its natural exit, either combines with the benefit of reduction another benefit or not. The first either that benefit is the generation of a body connected to the body of the same kind as the limbs, which is the substance of the nail, or not, which is the substance of hair, or disconnected, which is the substance of the child, that is, semen; or it is not the generation of another body. Then that benefit sometimes relates to semen, like the prostatic fluid that eases its exit, and sometimes relates to the fetus during its formation like menses, or during its exit like the fluids occurring during childbirth, or after that like milk; and sometimes does not relate to either of them. That is either to repel the harm of something exiting from the body, like the prostatic fluid that breaks by its acidity the sharpness of urine, or entering it like earwax that kills by its bitterness what enters it from flies and like it; or not to repel the harm of something, like saliva that helps speech by moistening the tongue. The second, which does not combine with the benefit of reduction another benefit, either a separate other body is formed from it like the substance of lice, or not separate like the substance of kidney stones; or nothing is formed, and it is either not sensible at all, like the evaporating vapor, or sensible sometimes like the body's dirt from the surplus of its nutrition, for it is not sensed unless collected or always; and its exit is either from a sensible outlet like mucus or from an insensible one like sweat.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of fluids that exit the body during the third digestion (related to nutrition). The author categorizes them first by completeness of transformation: incomplete (pus, purulent) or complete. Complete transformation leads either to a state suitable for nutrition (like certain urine sediment) or not (like discharge from abscesses). Then he focuses on fluids that exit naturally. They either combine the benefit of reducing excess with another benefit or not. If they have another benefit, that benefit may be generating a connected body part (nail, hair), a disconnected part (semen), or not generating a body. Benefits relating to semen, fetus, or childbirth are discussed. Some fluids repel harm (e.g., prostatic fluid neutralizing urine acidity; earwax killing insects). Others aid functions like saliva aiding speech. If no additional benefit, the fluid may form another body (lice, kidney stones) or not. Non-forming fluids may be insensible (vapor) or sensible (body dirt). Exit is through sensible outlets (mucus) or insensible ones (sweat). The classification is exhaustive and reflects a medicinal view of bodily economy.

Key Terms
هضم ثالث
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two types of transformation that fluids from the third digestion can undergo?
A) Incomplete and complete
B) Natural and unnatural
C) Nutritious and non-nutritious
D) Sensible and insensible

Key Takeaway
The third digestion produces fluids that can be classified by transformation type, with natural exits either solely for reduction or combined with other benefits.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that even waste products like pus or blood from a nosebleed are part of a divinely designed system with order and purpose. When you experience such things in your own body, do you pause to recognize the wisdom behind them, or do you merely react with annoyance? What would it take for you to turn these moments into opportunities for gratitude and reflection on Allah's intricate creation?
?
If even the products of the third digestion can serve a beneficial purpose (like nutrition), what does that suggest about the potential hidden benefits in the painful or 'wasteful' experiences of your spiritual journey? Are there setbacks or trials you have been quick to dismiss as useless? How can you actively seek the benefit Allah may have placed within them?
?
The classification mentions fluids that exit naturally either for reduction only or for reduction plus another benefit. In your life, how often do you reduce (let go of) something—time, energy, relationships—without considering that there might be an additional benefit? Ask yourself: Is there something I am simply discarding that Allah might want me to repurpose for a greater good? How can I change my perspective to seek that extra benefit?

The Benefits of Bodily Fluids: Generation, Protection, and Other Functions
Classifies natural-exit fluids by additional benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed classification of bodily fluids that naturally exit with an additional benefit versus those without, including examples such as nails, hair, semen, menses, milk, earwax, saliva, lice, kidney stones, mucus, and sweat.

Core Idea
Every fluid that leaves the body serves a purpose, from generating new life to protecting against harm.

After establishing the basic classification, the author delves into the rich details of fluids that combine the benefit of reduction with another benefit. This lesson explores the various subcategories: fluids that generate body parts (nails, hair), those related to reproduction (semen, menses, milk), those that repel harm (earwax, prostatic fluid), and those aiding functions (saliva). Then it covers fluids without additional benefit, which may form separate bodies (lice, kidney stones) or nothing, and exit through sensible or insensible outlets.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the entire passage for lesson 2, noting the branching structure.
2
Identify the main branches
Find the two main categories: fluids with additional benefit and those without. Within the first, note the subcategories: generating connected body, generating disconnected body, not generating a body but relating to semen/fetus/childbirth, and those repelling harm or aiding speech.
The classification is exhaustive.
3
Analyze examples
Consider each example: nails, hair, semen, menses, milk, prostatic fluid, earwax, saliva, lice, kidney stones, vapor, body dirt, mucus, sweat. Understand why each is placed where.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Think about the medical and theological implications: the body's design ensures that even waste products serve multiple purposes.
This aligns with Islamic view of creation.

Regarding the third digestion, like hemorrhoid blood, and the spoiled blood exiting through nosebleed and other than it; or it may have undergone incomplete transformation like pus and purulent discharge, or complete transformation: either to a state suitable for nutrition, like the ripe [؟] exiting from urine in a state of health, missing the nutritive yellowness, or not, like the discharge exiting from bursting abscesses. Inductive Proof for the Four Humors
Inductive proof by observing blood components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four humors are identified through inductive observation of blood components.

Core Idea
The four humors are directly observed in blood as foam, sediment, and egg-white.

The author begins by asserting that the four humors result from digestion and are distributed via veins. He then provides an inductive proof: by examining blood, we find it always contains three distinct components, each corresponding to a humor. This empirical grounding establishes the humoral theory on observable evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author presents the inductive argument as a direct observation.
The passage uses the term 'istiqrā' (induction) to describe the method.
2
Identify the three components
Look for the three substances described: foam, sediment, and egg-white. Match each to its corresponding humor.
Foam = yellow bile, sediment = black bile, egg-white = phlegm.
3
Reflect on induction as proof
Consider why the author considers this observation a valid proof. What does it show about the humors?
Induction relies on repeated empirical observation, which grounds theory in experience.

قال فتصير الأخلاط الأربعة ثم يندفع في العروق، ويتميز ما يليق لكل عضو، ويرشح عليهم من فوهات العروق الكثيفة. يعني الدم والبلغم والصفراء والسوداء، وذلك بحكم الاستقراء، فإن الحيوان سواء كان صحيحاً أو مريضاً، يجد دمه مخالطاً لشيء كالرغوة وهو الصفراء، أو لشيء كالرسوب وهو السوداء، أو لشيء كبياض البيض وهو البلغم، وما هذه الثلاثة فهو الدم، وقد يقال : إن الكيلوس إذا انطبخ، فإن كان معتدلاً فالدم، وإن كان قاصراً فالبلغم والسوداء، وإن كان مفرطاً فالصفراء. وأيضاً فإن الأخلاط تتكون من الأغذية المركبة من الإسطقسات الأربعة، فبحسب غلبة قوة واحد واحد منها يوجد خلط خلط . وأيضاً الغذاء شبيه بالمغتذي، وإن في البدن عضواً بارداً يابساً كالعظم، وبارداً رطباً كالدماغ ، وحاراً رطباً كالكبد، وحاراً يابساً كالقلب، فيجب أن تكون الأخلاط كذلك ليغتذي كل عضو بما يناسبه.
He said: So the four humors result, then they are propelled into the veins, and what is suitable for each organ is distinguished, and they filter upon them from the openings of the thick veins. Meaning blood, phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile. That is by induction, for the animal whether healthy or sick finds its blood mixed with something like foam, which is yellow bile, or something like sediment, which is black bile, or something like egg white, which is phlegm, and what is other than these three is blood. And it is said: When chyle is cooked, if it is moderate then blood, if deficient then phlegm and black bile, if excessive then yellow bile. Also, the humors are formed from foods composed of the four elements, so according to the predominance of the power of each one, each humor exists. Also, food resembles that which is nourished, and in the body there is a cold dry organ like bone, cold moist like brain, hot moist like liver, hot dry like heart, so it is necessary that the humors be likewise so that each organ is nourished by what suits it.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of the four humors and provides three proofs. First, by induction (istiqrā'): observation of blood reveals three components: foam (yellow bile), sediment (black bile), and egg-white-like substance (phlegm); the remainder is blood. Second, by analogy with cooking: chyle (kaylūs) when cooked moderately yields blood; undercooked yields phlegm and black bile; overcooked yields yellow bile. Third, by the principle that food resembles what it nourishes: since the body has organs with opposite temperaments (cold-dry bone, cold-moist brain, hot-moist liver, hot-dry heart), the humors must also have corresponding temperaments to nourish each organ appropriately. The passage follows a logical sequence: first the statement of the four humors, then the inductive argument, then the cooking analogy, then the organ-humor correspondence. It demonstrates a systematic approach to proving medical principles within a theological context.

Key Terms
الدم
البلغم
الصفراء
السوداء
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four humors are purely theoretical and not observable.
Correction: The passage shows that they are directly observable in blood as distinct components: foam, sediment, and egg-white.

Checkpoint
According to the inductive argument, what does foam in blood correspond to?
A) Blood
B) Phlegm
C) Yellow bile
D) Black bile

Key Takeaway
The four humors are empirically observed in blood as distinct components, providing an inductive proof for their existence.

Analogical Proofs: Cooking and Organ Correspondence
Analogical proofs: cooking of chyle and organ temperament correspondence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the analogical proofs for the four humors based on the cooking of chyle and the temperament of organs.

Core Idea
Humors arise from cooking food and must match organ temperaments.

After the inductive proof, the author offers two analogical arguments. The first compares humor formation to cooking chyle: moderate cooking yields blood, while deviations produce other humors. The second invokes the principle that food must resemble what it nourishes: since organs have different temperaments, the humors must correspond in kind. These analogies provide additional causal support for the humoral theory.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogies
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice there are two separate analogies: cooking of chyle and the food-nourished resemblance.
The second analogy also invokes the four elements (isṭuqussāt) composing food.
2
Analyze the cooking analogy
Understand the three outcomes: moderate cooking gives blood; deficient gives phlegm and black bile; excessive gives yellow bile.
This links humor production to a process of cooking or digestion.
3
Analyze the organ correspondence
The principle 'food resembles the nourished' implies that humors must match organ temperaments. The four organs listed have opposite qualities.
Bone: cold-dry; Brain: cold-moist; Liver: hot-moist; Heart: hot-dry.
4
Synthesize the proofs
Consider how both analogies reinforce the idea that the four humors are necessary and natural.

Blood: The True Nutrient Among Humors
Clarifies the hierarchical position of blood among humors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Galenic medicine, blood is the primary nutrient while the other three humors act as correctives.

Core Idea
Blood is the true nutrient; other humors are like spices.

The passage begins by addressing a common misconception: although there are four humors, they do not have equal nutritive value. The author states categorically that blood alone is the genuine nutrient; the other humors are merely adjustive, like spices that perfect a dish. This hierarchy explains why blood is described as the best in temperament, consistency, and taste.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Read the Arabic sentence from the beginning up to 'وألذها طعماً'. Notice the emphatic assertion (والحق أن) and the metaphor of corrective spices (كالأبازير المصلحة).
The word 'أبازير' means spices or seasonings.
2
Identify the main point
What does the author say about blood compared to the other humors? He says blood is the real nutrient (الغاذي بالحقيقة) and the others are like correctives. Then he lists three reasons why blood is superior.
3
Reflect on the metaphor
Think about the function of spices: they enhance but do not provide the main substance. Similarly, the other humors modify and balance blood but are not themselves primary nutrients.
This metaphor reinforces the central role of blood in nutrition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا والحق أن الغاذي بالحقيقة هو الدم وباقي الأخلاط كالأبازير المصلحة ، ولهذا كان أفضل الأخلاط، وأعدلها مزاجاً ،وقواماً، وألذها طعماً، وفسروا الخلط بأنه رطب سيال، يستحيل إليه الغذاء أولا ، واحترز بالرطب، أي سهل القبول للتشكل عند عدم مانع من خارج عن مثل العظم والغضروف، وبالسيال أي ما من شأنه أن ينبسط أجزاء متسفلة بالطبع، حيث لا مانع عن مثل اللحم والشحم ، إن قلنا بكونهما رطبيين والمراد بالاستحالة التغير في الجوهر بحرارة البدن، وتصرف الغاذية بقرينة التعدية بإلى . جسم إذ يقال في العرف استحال الماء إلى الهواء، وقلما يقال. استحال الماء الحار إلى الباردة، بل بارداً وبه احترز عن الكيلوس الذي يستحيل إليه الغذاء أولا في كيفيته والمراد بالغذاء ما هو المتعارف من مثل اللحم والخبز، وسائر ما
This, and the truth is that the nutrient in reality is blood, and the rest of the humors are like corrective spices; therefore it is the best of humors, the most balanced in temperament, consistency, and most delicious in taste. And they interpreted the humor as a moist fluid (rāṭib sayyāl) that the food first transforms into. And by 'moist' he meant easily accepting formation when there is no external impediment, unlike bone and cartilage; and by 'fluid' he meant that its parts naturally tend to spread downwards, where no impediment exists, unlike flesh and fat if we say they are moist. And by transformation (istiḥāla) is meant change in substance by the body's heat and the action of the nutritive faculty, indicated by the transitive particle 'ilā. For it is said in common usage: water transformed into air, and it is rarely said: hot water transformed into cold, but rather becomes cold. And by it he guarded against chyle which the food first transforms into in its quality. And by 'food' is meant what is commonly known, like meat and bread, and all that which
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage corrects a common misunderstanding: although there are four humors, the true nutrient is blood; the other humors are like spices that correct or adjust. Blood is therefore the best humor in terms of temperament, consistency, and taste. The passage then provides a technical definition of 'khalt' (humor): it is a moist fluid (rāṭib sayyāl) that food first transforms into. The definition is carefully qualified: 'moist' (rāṭib) means easily shaped when no external hindrance exists, unlike bone or cartilage which are dry. 'Fluid' (sayyāl) means parts naturally tend to spread downwards, unlike flesh or fat if considered moist. 'Transformation' (istiḥāla) means a change in substance caused by the body's heat and the nutritive faculty, indicated by the particle 'ilā (to) as in 'water transformed to air'—note that it is not a change in quality (like hot to cold) but in substance. This distinguishes humor from chyle (kaylūs), which is only a qualitative change. Finally, 'food' is defined in the common sense (meat, bread, etc.). The passage thus gives a precise, technical definition of a key medical term.

الغاذي
الخلط
الأبازير المصلحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All four humors are equally nutritive.
Correction: The passage states that blood alone is the true nutrient; the other humors are like corrective spices, meaning they assist but are not primary.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship of the other humors to blood?
A) They are equally nutritive.
B) They are like corrective spices that adjust blood.
C) They are waste products.
D) They are the main nutrients.

Key Takeaway
Blood is the true nutrient; other humors are auxiliary correctives, not primary sources of nourishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Precise Definition of Khalt as a Moist Fluid
Defines 'khalt' and qualifies each term with conditions and exclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of 'khalt' (humor) as a moist fluid and the specific conditions attached to each component of the definition.

Core Idea
A humor is a moist fluid that food first transforms into.

After establishing blood's primacy, the passage provides a rigorous definition of the term 'khalt' itself. The definition is carefully qualified: 'moist' (rāṭib) means easily shaped when no external impediment exists, unlike bone; 'fluid' (sayyāl) means its parts naturally spread downward, unlike flesh; 'transformation' (istiḥāla) means a change in substance caused by the body's heat, not a mere qualitative change like that of chyme (kaylūs). Finally, 'food' is defined in the everyday sense. This precision ensures the term is not confused with other bodily substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the full definition
Read the Arabic from 'وفسروا الخلط' to the end. Note that it defines khalt as a moist fluid (رطب سيال) and then explains each word with conditions and exceptions.
The definition is: 'a moist fluid that food first transforms into'.
2
Understand 'moist' (rāṭib)
The author clarifies that 'moist' means the substance is easily changeable in shape when no external hindrance is present. This excludes things like bone and cartilage, which are dry and resistant.
Moist here is about plasticity, not wetness.
3
Understand 'fluid' (sayyāl)
A fluid is something whose parts naturally tend downwards (mutasaffila) unless impeded. This excludes flesh and fat (if considered moist), as they are not fluid.
Note the distinction between being moist and being fluid.
4
Understand 'transformation' (istiḥāla)
Transformation here means a change in the substance itself, caused by the body's heat and the nutritive faculty. The particle 'ilā indicates a change into something else, like water to air. This excludes mere qualitative change, which is what happens to chyme (kaylūs).
The passage uses the common example: 'water transformed into air', not 'hot water transformed into cold'.
5
Grasp the overall purpose
The detailed conditions ensure that the definition of khalt is precise and excludes other substances like chyme, bone, cartilage, flesh, etc. It shows the technical nature of medical terminology.

Source Text

A humor is a moist fluid that food substantially transforms into, with precise conditions excluding other bodily substances like chyle, bone, and cartilage.

?

?

What would it mean for you to undergo a true istiḥāla (transformation in substance) in your relationship with your Creator?

The Four Humors: Existence and Evidence
Sanusi presents three arguments for the four humors and asserts blood's primacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and empirical evidence for the existence of four distinct humors in the body.

Core Idea
The four humors are known by induction, chyle digestion, and the body's own composition.

Sanusi opens by presenting multiple lines of evidence for the four humors: from direct observation of animal blood, from the transformation of chyle, and from the correspondence with the body's members. This lesson walks through each proof and explains why blood is considered the primary humor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the four humors
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overall sense of the arguments. Then read the translation.
Notice the use of induction and analogy.
2
Identify the three evidences
The passage gives three proofs: induction from animal blood, cooking of chyle, and correspondence with the four elements and body members. Find each one and note how they interrelate.
3
Understand the primacy of blood
After listing the humors, Sanusi asserts that blood is the true nourisher and the other humors are like condiments. Consider why this matters for the definition of khilt.
This sets up the formal definition in the next lesson.

يعني الدم والبلغم والصفراء والسوداء، وذلك بحكم الاستقراء، فإن الحيوان سواء كان صحيحاً أو مريضاً، يجد دمه مخالطاً لشيء كالرغوة وهو الصفراء، أو لشيء كالرسوب وهو السوداء، أو لشيء كبياض البيض وهو البلغم، وما هذه الثلاثة فهو الدم، وقد يقال : إن الكيلوس إذا انطبخ، فإن كان معتدلاً فالدم، وإن كان قاصراً فالبلغم والسوداء، وإن كان مفرطاً فالصفراء. وأيضاً فإن الأخلاط تتكون من الأغذية المركبة من الإسطقسات الأربعة، فبحسب غلبة قوة واحد واحد منها يوجد خلط خلط . وأيضاً الغذاء شبيه بالمغتذي، وإن في البدن عضواً بارداً يابساً كالعظم، وبارداً رطباً كالدماغ، وحاراً رطباً كالكبد، وحاراً يابساً كالقلب، فيجب أن تكون الأخلاط كذلك ليغتذي كل عضو بما يناسبه هذا والحق أن الغاذي بالحقيقة هو الدم وباقي الأخلاط كالأبازير المصلحة، ولهذا كان أفضل الأخلاط، وأعدلها مزاجاً، وقواماً، وألذها طعماً، وفسروا الخلط بأنه رطب سيال، يستحيل إليه الغذاء أولا، واحترز بالرطب، أي سهل القبول للتشكل عند عدم مانع من خارج عن مثل العظم والغضروف، وبالسيال أي ما من شأنه أن ينبسط أجزاء متسفلة بالطبع، حيث لا مانع عن مثل اللحم والشحم، إن قلنا بكونهما رطبيين والمراد بالاستحالة التغير في الجوهر بحرارة البدن، وتصرف الغاذية بقرينة التعدية بإلى. إذ يقال في العرف استحال الماء إلى الهواء، وقلما يقال استحال الماء الحار إلى الباردة، بل بارداً وبه احترز عن الكيلوس الذي يستحيل إليه الغذاء أولا في كيفيته والمراد بالغذاء ما هو المتعارف من مثل اللحم والخبز، وسائر ما يرد على البدن فيغذوه واحترز بقيد الأولية عن الرطوبات الثانية وعن المني، فإن الغذاء إنما يستحيل إليهما بعد الاستحالة إلى الخلط، ويرد عليه إشكال بالخلط المتولد من الخلط كالدم من البلغم ويدفع بأن المراد استحالة الغذاء أولا في الجملة. وكل خلط فرض، فإن من شأنه أن الغذاء يستحيل إليه أولا. ثم لا خفاء في أنه مثل اللحم والعظم وجميع ما عدا الخلط يخرج بهذا القيد، فذكر الرطب والسيال يكون مستدركاً، بل مخلا بالانعكاس، إذ يخرج البلغم الجصي، والسوداء الرمادية فإنهما غير سيالين بحكم المشاهدة، والقول بأن عدم السيلان لمانع ليس بقادح ضعيف.
Meaning blood, phlegm, yellow bile, and black bile. This is by way of induction, for the animal, whether healthy or sick, finds its blood mixed with something like foam, which is yellow bile, or something like sediment, which is black bile, or something like egg white, which is phlegm, and what remains of these three is blood. And it is said: when chyle is cooked, if it is moderate it becomes blood, if deficient it becomes phlegm and black bile, if excessive it becomes yellow bile. Also, the humors are generated from foods composed of the four elements; according to the predominance of the power of each one, each humor exists. Also, food is similar to what is nourished, and in the body there is a cold dry member like bone, cold moist like the brain, hot moist like the liver, hot dry like the heart, so the humors must be likewise so that each member is nourished by what suits it. The truth is that the nourisher in reality is blood, and the remaining humors are like spices that improve it; for this reason it is the best of humors, the most balanced in temperament, consistency, and the most delicious in taste. And they defined khilt as a fluid moisture that flows, to which food is first transformed. By 'moist' they meant easily accepting shape when there is no external impediment, like bone and cartilage; by 'flowing' they meant that which naturally spreads its parts downward when there is no impediment, like flesh and fat, if we say they are moist. By 'transformation' they meant change in substance by the heat of the body and the action of the nutritive faculty, by virtue of the transitive preposition 'ila'. For it is said customarily: water transformed into air, but it is rarely said: hot water transformed into cold, rather it is said: became cold. By this he avoids chyle, to which food is first transformed in its quality. By 'food' is meant what is conventionally known, like meat, bread, and everything that enters the body and nourishes it. By the qualification 'first' he avoids secondary moistures and semen, for food transforms into these only after transformation into khilt. An objection arises concerning khilt generated from khilt, like blood from phlegm; the answer is that what is meant is the first transformation of food in general. And every assumed khilt is such that food is first transformed into it. Then it is obvious that like meat, bone, and everything other than khilt are excluded by this qualification. Thus mentioning 'moist' and 'flowing' is redundant, indeed detrimental to proper inclusion, for it excludes gypsum-like phlegm and ash-like black bile, for these are not flowing by observation, and the claim that their lack of flow is due to an impediment is not a valid objection; it is weak.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines the four humors (blood, phlegm, yellow bile, black bile) and gives the technical definition of 'khilt' (humor). It begins by demonstrating the existence of the four humors through induction: in animals, blood is always found mixed with substances that correspond to the other three humors. It then provides additional arguments: the cooking of chyle produces different humors depending on moderation or excess; foods composed of the four elements generate humors according to which element predominates; and the body's members have different temperaments (cold-dry, cold-moist, etc.), so the humors must similarly vary to nourish each member appropriately. The author then asserts that blood is the true nourisher and the other humors are like condiments that improve it. The formal definition of khilt is given: 'a fluid moisture that flows, to which food is first transformed.' Each part of the definition is explained: 'moist' means easily shapeable; 'flowing' means naturally spreading downward; 'transformation' means a change in substance caused by the body's heat and the nutritive faculty. The definition is then qualified to exclude chyle (which is a first transformation in quality, not substance), secondary moistures, and semen. An objection is raised: khilt can be generated from another khilt (e.g., blood from phlegm), so how can each be the first recipient? The answer is that what is meant is the first transformation of food in general, not that every khilt must be directly from food. Finally, the author criticizes the definition, arguing that the qualifications 'moist' and 'flowing' are actually redundant and cause the definition to fail to include some types of phlegm and black bile that are not flowing, and the excuse that this is due to an external impediment is weak.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four humors are arbitrary categories without empirical basis.
Correction: Sanusi provides three empirical arguments: direct observation of blood mixtures, the cooking of chyle, and the correspondence with bodily temperatures and elements.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what happens when chyle is cooked in a deficient manner?
A) It becomes blood.
B) It becomes phlegm and black bile.
C) It becomes yellow bile.
D) It becomes semen.

Key Takeaway
The existence of four humors is supported by multiple lines of evidence, with blood being the primary humor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi uses three distinct observations to prove the existence of the four humors. In your own faith, do you base your beliefs on observation and reflection, or do you accept them without question? Where might you be neglecting the evidence placed before you? Will you commit to examining one core belief this week with the same inductive rigor?
?
Blood is described as the primary nourisher, with the other humors as mere 'spices.' What is the 'blood' of your spiritual life—the essential substance that sustains you—and what are the 'spices' that you may be overemphasizing? Are you in danger of mistaking the seasoning for the substance?
?
The body's members are nourished by humors that suit their temperament. Consider your own soul: what foods—knowledge, companions, actions—are you feeding it? Do they match your true spiritual needs, or are you consuming things that leave you imbalanced? What will you change today?

The Technical Definition of Khilt
Sanusi gives the formal definition of khilt with its conditions, exclusions, and critiques.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of khilt as the first transformation of food and the objections that refine this definition.

Core Idea
Khilt is defined as the first fluid transformation of food, excluding chyle, secondary moistures, and other tissues.

After establishing the four humors, Sanusi turns to the formal definition of a humor (khilt). This definition is carefully worded to exclude other bodily fluids and tissues. The lesson unpacks each component of the definition and examines an objection and a critique of the definition's wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its explanation
Read the Arabic definition of khilt and the detailed explanation of each term: 'moist', 'flowing', and 'first transformation'.
Pay attention to what each qualifier excludes.
2
Identify what is excluded
The definition explicitly excludes chyle (transformation in quality only), secondary moistures, and semen. Note why these are excluded.
3
Examine the objection and answer
An objection says that khilt can be generated from another khilt (e.g., blood from phlegm). Sanusi answers that 'first' means in general, not for every instance.
4
Consider the critique
Sanusi criticizes the inclusion of 'moist' and 'flowing' as redundant and even detrimental, because some types of phlegm and black bile are not flowing. Evaluate this critique.
This shows a critical engagement with the definition.

The Four Aspects of Assimilation and Their Impairments
Sanusi enumerates the four aspects of food assimilation and their corresponding defects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of tashabbuh (assimilation) of food into body members, its four aspects—color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion—and the medical conditions that arise when these aspects are impaired.

Core Idea
Assimilation is the full incorporation of food into a member, not just superficial similarity.

In this passage, Sanusi explains the precise process by which food becomes an actual part of a body member. He identifies four essential aspects—color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion—and then shows how certain diseases represent failures in these aspects. This connects theological anthropology to medical theory, illustrating the harmony between bodily function and divine design.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own words establish the framework.
2
Identify the four aspects
Find the four aspects mentioned: color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion. Notice that they are listed together at the start.
These are the pillars of assimilation.
3
Understand the definition
Sanusi defines assimilation as becoming a part of the member in natural proportion, without remaining separate or flabby. Grasp this core idea.
The opposite is fleshy dropsy, which remains distinct.
4
Examine the impairments
The passage lists three diseases that impair specific aspects: fleshy dropsy (adhesion), vitiligo/leucoderma (color), and withering (consistency). Note how each corresponds to a failure.
Temperament is not given a specific impairment here.
5
Reflect on incomplete consistency
Finally, Sanusi notes that if consistency is incomplete, the transformed substance is like a spray-like moisture that has not yet hardened. Consider how this fits the overall process.
This shows the gradual nature of assimilation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ قال (ثم يتشبه به) لوناً، وقواماً، ومزاجاً، والتصاقاً. أي يصير ما يليق بالعضو، ويرشح عليه شبيهاً به في المزاج والقوام واللون والالتصاق، أعني صيرورته جزءاً من العضو على النسبة الطبيعية، من غير أن يبقى عنه، مترهلا كما في الاستسقاء اللحمي. فإن ذلك إخلال بفعل الالصاق. كما أن البرص والبهق إخلال بالتشبه في اللون. أما الذبول فإخلال بتحصيل ومن الإخلال بالفعل ما وقع في المواقف. أن الاستسقاء اللحمي جوهر متميزاً عنه، الغذاء، ومن إخلال بالقوام، والذبول إخلال بالالتصاق، ولا أدري كيف يقع مثله لمثله. واعلم أنه إذا لم يكمل القوام فهي رطوبة رذاذية طلية، قد التصقت بالعضو، وانعقدت، واستحالت إليه من جهة المزاج، لكن لقرب عهدها بالانعقاد لم تصلب بعد، ولم يحصل لها قوام العضو.
[He said: then it assimilates to it (tashabbuh) in color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion.] That is, it becomes what is suitable for the member, and it exudes upon it something similar to it in temperament, consistency, color, and adhesion, meaning its becoming a part of the member in the natural proportion, without remaining separate from it, flabby as in fleshy dropsy (istisqa' lahm). For that is an impairment of the act of adhesion. Just as vitiligo (baras) and leucoderma (bahaq) are impairments of assimilation in color. As for withering (dhubul), it is an impairment of the acquisition (tahsil) [of food?]. And among the impairments of the act is what occurs in the Mawaqif. That fleshy dropsy is a substance distinct from it, the food, and it is an impairment of consistency, and withering is an impairment of adhesion, and I do not know how such a thing could occur for such a thing. Know that if the consistency is not complete, then it is a spray-like moisture that has adhered to the member, coagulated, and transformed into it in terms of temperament, but because its time of coagulation is recent, it has not yet hardened, and it has not acquired the consistency of the member.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of 'tashabbuh' (assimilation), the process by which food becomes like a body member and is incorporated into it. The author states that assimilation occurs in four aspects: color, consistency (qiwam), temperament (mizaj), and adhesion (iltisaq). In other words, the food becomes suitable for the member and is transformed into a part of it while maintaining the natural proportion, without remaining as a separate flabby substance, like in the case of fleshy dropsy (istisqa' lahm). The author then discusses impairments of this process: a lack of proper adhesion leads to fleshy dropsy; a lack of color assimilation leads to skin diseases like vitiligo (baras) and leucoderma (bahaq); and a lack of consistency leads to withering (dhubul). The author mentions that in al-Mawaqif (likely a reference to another text), it is stated that fleshy dropsy involves a substance distinct from the food, impairing consistency, while withering impairs adhesion. He expresses uncertainty about how such a condition occurs. Finally, he notes that if the consistency is incomplete, the transformed substance remains a spray-like moisture that has adhered and coagulated but has not yet achieved the full consistency of the member due to its recent formation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assimilation is merely a superficial similarity in appearance.
Correction: Assimilation involves actual incorporation as a part of the member with the same temperament and consistency, not just superficial similarity.

Checkpoint
What are the four aspects in which food assimilates to a body member according to this passage?
A) Color, taste, smell, and texture.
B) Color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion.
C) Size, shape, weight, and density.
D) Heat, cold, moisture, and dryness.

Key Takeaway
Assimilation (tashabbuh) is the complete integration of food into a body member in color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion, and its failure leads to specific diseases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that true assimilation requires complete integration in color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion. In your own life, are there areas where you have only superficially 'assimilated' something important without it becoming a real part of you? What specific aspect is missing—the external appearance, firmness, inner disposition, or genuine connection? Commit to one step to move from mere transformation to true assimilation.
?
The passage shows that impairment in any aspect of assimilation leads to a disease. Consider your spiritual 'food'—what you read, listen to, or practice. Is your assimilation of beneficial knowledge complete, or do you suffer from a 'spiritual dropsy' where the knowledge remains separate and flabby? Reflect on one aspect that needs strengthening and make a sincere intention to address it.
?
You have read that assimilation requires becoming suitable for the member in the natural proportion. How often do you accept teachings that are not proportionate to your own capacity or fit your inner constitution? How can you ensure that what you take in truly fits you and becomes part of you?

Definition of the First Transformation of Food into the Primary Humor
Definition of the first transformation with objection and rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'first' in the transformation of food into the primary humor, and how the author defends his definition against the objection of humor-to-humor transformation.

Core Idea
The 'first' transformation means food becomes a humor before becoming anything else.

In this passage, the author precisely defines the first stage of food transformation. He not only clarifies what counts as 'first' but also defends his definition against a logical objection. This lesson unpacks the argument and explains why the definition excludes secondary moistures and non-fluid humors.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of 'first transformation'
Read the passage carefully. Note that 'first' is a condition that excludes secondary moistures and sperm because food transforms into them only after becoming a humor.
The author uses the term 'الأولية' to indicate this priority.
2
Examine the objection and its rebuttal
Identify the objection: humor generated from humor (e.g., blood from phlegm). Then see how the author rebuts by clarifying that 'first' applies to each humor type individually.
The rebuttal emphasizes that for any assumed humor, food becomes that humor first in its own category.
3
Analyze why 'moist and fluid' would be problematic
The author argues that adding 'moist and fluid' to the definition would exclude non-fluid humors like gypsum-like phlegm and ash-like black bile. The counterclaim that non-fluidity is due to a hindrance is dismissed as weak.
This shows the author's careful reasoning to ensure the definition is accurate and inclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرد على البدن فيغذوه واحترز بقيد الأولية عن الرطوبات الثانية وعن المني، فإن الغذاء إنما يستحيل إليهما بعد الاستحالة إلى الخلط، ويرد عليه إشكال بالخلط المتولد من الخلط كالدم من البلغم ويدفع بأن المراد استحالة الغذاء أولا (۱) في الجملة . وكل خلط فرض، فإن من شأنه أن الغذاء يستحيل إليه أولا . ثم لا خفاء في أنه مثل اللحم والعظم وجميع ما عدا الخلط يخرج بهذا القيد، فذكر الرطب والسيال يكون مستدركاً، بل مخلا بالانعكاس (۲)، إذ يخرج البلغم الجصي ، والسوداء الرمادية فإنهما غير سيالين بحكم المشاهدة، والقول بأن عدم السيلان لمانع ليس بقادح ضعيف .
It returns to the body and nourishes it, and he restricted by the condition of firstness to secondary moistures and to sperm, for food only changes into them after changing into the humor. An objection is raised against him concerning the humor generated from a humor, like blood from phlegm, and he repels it by saying that what is meant is the first transformation of food in general. And every humor assumed, it is in its nature that food changes into it first. Then it is not hidden that things like flesh and bone and all except the humor are excluded by this condition. So mentioning 'moist' and 'fluid' would be redundant, rather it disrupts the inversion, since it excludes gypsum-like phlegm and ash-like black bile, for they are not fluid by observation. And the statement that non-fluidity is due to a hindrance is a weak objection.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines the first transformation of food into the primary humor. The author explains that the term 'first' excludes secondary moistures (like sperm) because food transforms into them only after becoming a humor. An objection: what about humors generated from other humors (e.g., blood from phlegm)? The answer: 'first' means that for any assumed humor, food changes into it first in its own category. Then he argues that limiting the definition to 'moist and fluid' would be incorrect, as it would exclude non-fluid humors like gypsum-like phlegm and ash-like black bile. The claim that their non-fluidity is accidental is weak.

Key Terms
الأولية
الرطوبات الثانية
المني
الخلط
الاستحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'first' refers to chronological order among all humors, meaning food becomes a single primary humor before turning into others.
Correction: In reality, 'first' applies within each humor type: for any humor, food becomes that humor first, regardless of later transformations.

Checkpoint
Why does the author exclude secondary moistures and sperm from the first transformation?
A) Because they are not fluid.
B) Because they are not humors.
C) Because they occur only after transformation into humor.
D) Because they are not food.

Key Takeaway
The first transformation of food is its change into the primary humor; all other transformations are subsequent and excluded by the condition of 'firstness'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author insists on precision with the term 'first' to avoid confusion. In what areas of your own understanding—whether in faith, practice, or daily decisions—do you tend to overlook such precision? How might that lack of clarity mislead you?
?
When a strong objection is raised against something you believe, do you immediately dismiss it with a weak counter like 'that is due to a hindrance,' or do you seek a thorough rebuttal as this scholar does? Examine one belief you hold and consider whether you have adequately defended it against valid objections.

The Four Aspects of Food Assimilation into the Organ
The author explains the process of assimilation of food into the organ's likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four aspects of assimilation (color, consistency, temperament, adhesion) and how failures in each manifest as diseases.

Core Idea
Assimilation is not just about color but also texture, temperament, and firm attachment.

The author explains the process by which food becomes part of an organ, taking on its properties. He identifies four key aspects that must be matched: color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion. He then gives examples of diseases that result from failure in one or more of these aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four aspects
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice that the author lists four aspects explicitly: colour, consistency, temperament, and adhesion.
The word 'تشبه' means assimilation; the four aspects are in the first sentence.
2
Understand each aspect
For each aspect, consider what it means for food to become like the organ: colour (مشابهة في اللون), consistency (قوام: texture/ firmness), temperament (مزاج: hot/cold/moist/dry balance), adhesion (التصاق: becoming firmly attached as part of the organ).
The author explains that the food must become part of the organ 'in the natural proportion' without remaining flabby.
3
Examine the failures
The author gives examples: fleshy dropsy (استسقاء لحمي) as failure of adhesion; vitiligo (برص) and leukoderma (بهق) as failure in colour; withering (ذبول) as failure in consistency and adhesion. Note his comment on incomplete consistency leading to thin moisture.
This illustrates that assimilation must be complete in all four aspects for the food to properly nourish the organ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ثم يتشبه به) لوناً، وقواماً، ومزاجاً، والتصاقاً. أي يصير ما يليق بالعضو، ويرشح عليه شبيهاً به في المزاج والقوام(۳) واللون والالتصاق، أعني صيرورته جزءاً من العضو على النسبة الطبيعية، من غير أن يبقى عنه ، مترهلا كما في الاستسقاء اللحمي. فإن ذلك إخلال بفعل الالصاق. كما أن البرص (٤) والبهق إخلال بالتشبه في اللون. أما الذبول فإخلال بتحصيل ومن الإخلال بالفعل ما وقع في المواقف. أن الاستسقاء اللحمي جوهر متميزاً عنه ، الغذاء، ومن إخلال بالقوام، والذبول إخلال بالالتصاق، ولا أدري كيف يقع مثله لمثله. واعلم أنه إذا لم يكمل القوام فهي رطوبة رذاذية طلية، قد التصقت بالعضو، وانعقدت، واستحالت إليه من جهة المزاج، لكن لقرب عهدها بالانعقاد لم تصلب بعد، ولم يحصل لها قوام العضو.
He said: 'Then it assimilates to it in color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion.' That is, it becomes what is suitable for the organ, and develops upon it similar in temperament, consistency, color, and adhesion — meaning its becoming a part of the organ in the natural proportion, without remaining separate from it, flabby as in fleshy dropsy. For that is a failure in the act of adhesion. Just as vitiligo and leukoderma are failures in assimilation in color. As for withering, it is a failure in obtaining [?] and from failures in the act is what occurred in the Mawāqif: that fleshy dropsy is a substance distinct from it, the food, and from failure in consistency, and withering is failure in adhesion, and I do not know how such occurs for the like of him. And know that if the consistency is not complete, it is a thin painted moisture, attached to the organ and congealed and changed into it in terms of temperament, but due to recency of congealing it has not hardened and has not obtained the consistency of the organ.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines assimilation into four aspects: color, consistency (texture), temperament (degree of heat/cold/moisture), and adhesion (firm attachment). The author explains that the food becomes part of the organ proportionally, not remaining detached or flabby. He gives diseases as examples: fleshy dropsy (failure in adhesion), vitiligo and leukoderma (failure in color), withering (failure in consistency and adhesion). He then notes that if consistency is incomplete, the moisture remains thin and not fully hardened.

Key Terms
تشبه
لون
قوام
مزاج
التصاق
استسقاء لحمي
برص
بهق
ذبول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assimilation is only about the food taking on the color of the organ.
Correction: Assimilation involves four aspects: color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion. Failure in any one of these can lead to disease.

Checkpoint
What are the four aspects of assimilation mentioned?
A) Color, taste, smell, texture.
B) Color, consistency, temperament, adhesion.
C) Moisture, dryness, heat, cold.
D) Size, shape, weight, density.

Key Takeaway
Food assimilates into an organ by matching it in color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion; failures in these lead to specific diseases.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage shows that assimilation requires matching in color, consistency, temperament, and adhesion. In your spiritual life, what teaching or truth have you 'attached' to yourself but not allowed to actually change your temperament or consistency? What would it take for it to fully become part of you?
?
Fleshy dropsy is a failure in adhesion—the food remains flabby and separate. Are there areas where you have superficially accepted a truth (like a doctrine or moral principle) but it has not truly adhered to your heart, remaining a flabby extra rather than integrated into your character? How can you move from attachment to assimilation?
?
When consistency is incomplete, the food becomes a 'thin painted moisture'—congealed but not hardened. Do you have beliefs or practices that have been 'painted on' your life—present but not solid? What would it mean for them to harden into the very texture of your being?

Objection and Answer Regarding Excess Moisture
An objection about excess moisture in the transformed food is raised and answered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that excess moisture might prevent food from matching an organ's temperament and the counterargument that congealing alone can incorporate the moisture.

Core Idea
Transformation can occur through congealing alone, not necessarily by removing excess moisture.

In the previous lesson, we considered how food transforms into the substance of an organ. But a potential problem arises: if the food contains excess moisture, how can it match the organ's temperament? The author presents an objection and a clever answer using the natural processes of congealing by heat and cold.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection claims excess moisture must dissolve for proper transformation.
The objection is introduced by واعترض.
2
Understand the answer's mechanism
The answer proposes that transformation can occur through congealing (انعقاد) without dissolution. Grasp that congealing does not remove moisture but incorporates it into a new consistency.
The key term is انعقاد.
3
Reflect on the examples
Two examples are given: flesh from putrid blood (congealed by heat) and fat from moisture and oiliness (congealed by cold). Consider how these support the answer.
These show that congealing can produce different substances from different materials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض (۱) بأنها حينئذ لا تكون على مزاج العضو لما فيها من زيادة مائية لا بدّ من (۲) تحللها . وردّ بأنه يجوز أن تكون الاستحالة إلى قوام العضو لا بتحلل المائية، بل بمجرد الانعقاد كاللحم يتولد من منتن الدم، ويعقده الحر، والشحم من مائيته ودسميته، ويعقده البرد.
And it was objected that then it would not be on the temperament of the organ because of the excess moisture in it that necessarily must dissolve. And it was answered that it is possible that the transformation into the consistency of the organ is not by dissolution of moisture, but by mere congealing, just as flesh is generated from putrid blood and congealed by heat, and fat from its moisture and oiliness and congealed by cold.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage records an objection and its answer. The objection claims that if the transformed food contains excess moisture, it cannot match the organ's temperament and the moisture must be removed (dissolved). The answer: transformation can occur through congealing alone, without loss of moisture. Examples: flesh forms from putrid blood by heat, and fat from moisture and oiliness by cold. Thus, excess moisture does not necessarily need to dissolve.

Key Terms
اعتراض
مزاج
مائية
تحلل
انعقاد
لحم
شحم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any excess moisture must be eliminated for proper transformation into an organ's substance.
Correction: The author demonstrates that congealing can incorporate moisture without dissolution, as seen in the generation of flesh and fat.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection about excess moisture?
A) By denying the existence of excess moisture.
B) By saying the moisture dissolves naturally.
C) By stating that transformation can occur through mere congealing.
D) By claiming the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Excess moisture does not necessarily need to dissolve; congealing can incorporate it into the organ's substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you assume that flaws or 'excess' in yourself must be removed entirely before you can be useful to Allah—when perhaps He wants to congeal and transform them instead? What is one thing you have been trying to eliminate that you could instead submit to His divine process of congealing?
?
The passage uses opposites—heat for flesh, cold for fat—to show that transformation can occur through different means. Where in your life have you insisted that only one method (prayer, study, community) can change you, while ignoring that Allah may use a completely opposite path to refine you? What step can you take today to trust His unexpected ways?

The Considered Similarity and Three Types of Food
The author defines considered similarity and classifies food into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of considered similarity for health preservation and the distinction between actual, remote potential, and close potential food.

Core Idea
Similarity is judged when food becomes part of the organ, not before.

The author begins by setting the foundation for health preservation: the moment when food actually becomes part of the body. He then clarifies that 'food' can mean different things depending on its stage, and he classifies these stages into clear categories. Understanding this classification is key to applying the principle of similarity correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of considered similarity
Read the opening sentence carefully. Notice that similarity is tied to the moment food becomes part of an organ (actual food), not before.
2
Identify the three categories of food
The passage lists three meanings of 'food': actual, remote potential, and close potential. Find each one and note the examples given.
Remote potential: bread, meat. Close potential: humors, secondary moistures.
3
Understand the exclusion of chyme
Some scholars exclude chyme from the definition of food. Why might that be? Reflect on the criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والشاكلة المعتبرة بين الغذاء والمغتذى) في حفظ الصحة، هي التي تكون حال ما يصير جزءاً من العضو، إذ هو الغذاء بالفعل، وأما قبله فبالقوة على الاختلاف (۳) في القرب والبعد]. فيه إشارة إلى أمرين أحدهما : أن الغذاء قد يطلق على ما هو بالفعل. أعني الجسم الذي ورد على البدن، واستحال إلى الصورة العضوية، وصارت جزءاً منه شبيهاً به، لكن لم يحصل له القوام التام الذي للعضو للقطع ، بأنه لا يقال للأجزاء من العضو أنها غذاء له، وقد يطلق على ما هو بالقوة البعيدة، أعني الجسم الذي من شأنه، إذا ورد على البدن، وانفعل عن حرارته أن يستحيل إلى الغذاء بالفعل كالخبز واللحم أو القريبة أعني الجسم المعد في البدن لأن يصير غذاء بالفعل كالأخلاط وبعض الرطوبات الثانية (4) ، أعني التي تستحيل إليها الأخلاط، وهل تطلق على الكيلوس منعه بعضهم . وثانيهما: أن المراد بالشاكلة في قولهم حفظ الصحة تكون بالمشاكل، كما أن علاج المريض يكون بالمضاد، موافقة مزاج الغذاء، حين ما هو غذاء بالفعل لمزاج المغتذي، حتى أن غذاء صاحب المزاج الحار ينبغي أن يكون بارداً ، بحيث إذا تصرف فيه طبيعته، فصار غذاء بالفعل، استحال عن البرد، وصار حاراً مشاكلاً لجوهر بدنه، لا أن يكون حاراً مثل مزاجه، وإلا لصار عند الهضم أحر مما ينبغي، وأسقمه، وربما صار من
He said: 'And the considered similarity between food and the nourished body in preserving health is that which occurs at the moment it becomes a part of the organ, for it is food in actuality. Before that, it is potential, with difference in proximity and distance.' In it there is an indication to two matters: First, that 'food' may be applied to that which is actual, namely the body that came to the body and changed into the organic form and became a part of it resembling it, but did not obtain the complete consistency of the organ because it is not said of the parts of the organ that they are food for it. And it may be applied to that which is remote potential, namely the body that, when it comes to the body and is acted upon by its heat, changes into actual food, like bread and meat; or close potential, namely the body prepared in the body to become actual food, like the humors and some secondary moistures, namely those into which the humors change. And some prevented applying it to chyme. Second, that by 'similarity' in their saying 'health preservation is by similarity, just as treatment of the sick is by opposition,' is the agreement of the temperament of the food at the time it is actual food with the temperament of the nourished, so that the food of a hot-tempered person should be cold, so that when his nature acts on it and it becomes actual food, it changes from cold to hot, matching his body's substance, not that it be hot like his temperament, otherwise at digestion it would become hotter than appropriate and make him ill, and perhaps it would become from
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of 'considered similarity' (الشاكلة المعتبرة) for health preservation. The author states that similarity should be considered at the moment food becomes part of the organ (actual food), not before. He then makes two points. First: 'food' has three meanings: (1) actual food — the transformed part of an organ (but not the organ itself), (2) remote potential — bread, meat that will transform, (3) close potential — humors and secondary moistures already in the body. Some exclude chyme. Second: for health, the food's temperament when it is actual food should match the body's temperament. For a hot-tempered person, food should initially be cold so that, through digestion, it becomes warm and matches the body. If it were already hot, it would become excessively hot and cause harm.

Key Terms
شاكلة
حفظ الصحة
غذاء بالفعل
غذاء بالقوة
مشاكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think similarity should be assessed at the time of eating or before digestion.
Correction: The author specifies that similarity is considered at the moment the food becomes part of the organ (actual food), not at earlier stages.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of remote potential food?
A) Humors
B) Bread and meat
C) Secondary moistures
D) A part of the organ

Key Takeaway
Similarity for health preservation is judged only when food becomes actual part of the organ, and food can be actual, remote potential, or close potential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You eat bread daily. Has it ever occurred to you that until that bread becomes a literal part of your cells, it is not actually nourishing you? What does that reveal about the distance between what you 'consume' spiritually and what truly becomes part of your character?
?
The author says food is only considered similar when it has become part of the organ. Is there a truth you have heard many times but never allowed to become part of you? What would it take for that truth to actually nourish your soul?
?
Some scholars refused to call chyme 'food' because it had not yet fully transformed. Are there beliefs or habits in your life that are like chyme—inside you but not yet integrated? Will you leave them as half-digested or complete the transformation?
