Definition of Generative Power and Majority View
Definition of generative power and majority view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the generative power and the majority view that it only obtains and separates matter, while a separate form-giving power imparts dispositions.

Core Idea
The generative power provides raw material; another power shapes it into form.

In this passage, the author defines the generative power as responsible for preparing the matter for a new individual. He then contrasts the majority view, which assigns only material preparation to this power, with a detailed description of a separate form-giving power that actually shapes the embryo.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of generative power
Read the first sentence carefully: it describes the generative power as obtaining from food what can become a principle of another individual and separating it into parts.
Note the phrase 'تصير مثلاً بالفعل' – it becomes similar in actuality.
2
Identify the majority view
The text then states that the majority hold that the generative power only obtains matter and separates it, while giving dispositions is a distinct power called 'form' (صورة).
3
Examine the form-giving power
Read the description of the form-giving power: it obtains the seed, separates it into parts and dispositions, and after digestion and transformations, imparts forms, powers, and accidents suitable to the species.
This power is responsible for the actual formation of the embryo.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها المولدة) وهي التي تحصل من الغذاء ما يصلح مبدأ لشخص آخر من نوع المغتذى وتفصله إلى أجزاء مختلفة، ونفيدها الهيئات التي لها يصير مثلاً بالفعل والجمهور على أن المولدة هي التي تحصل المادة ،وتفصلها، ومحصل الهيئات قوة أخرى تسمى بصورة . وهي قوة شأنها تحصيل البذر وتفصيله إلى أجزاء مختلفة، وهيئات مناسبة، وذلك بأن تفرز من الغذاء بعد الهضم التام ليصير مبدأ لشخص آخر من نوع المغتذى أو جنسه ، ثم تفصل ما فيه من الكيفيات المزاجية، فتمزجها تمزيجات بحسب عضو عضو، ثم تفيده بعد الاستحالات الصور والقوى والأعراض الحاصلة للنوع الذي انفصل عند البذر أو لجنسه كما في البغل، والمحققون على أن هذه الأفعال مستندة إلى قوى ثلاث، بينوا حالها على ما عرف في الإنسان، وكثير من الحيوانات الأولى التي تجذب الدم إلى الأنثيين، وتتصرف فيه إلى أن يصير منياً، وهي لا تفارق الانثيين، وتخص باسم المحصلة، والثانية التي تتصرف في المنى، فتفصل كيفياتها المزاجية، وتمزجها تمزيجات بحسب عضو عضو، فتعين مثلاً للعصب مزاجاً خاصاً، وللشريان مزاجاً خاصاً، وللعظم مزاجاً خاصاً. وبالجملة تعد مواد الأعضاء وتخص هذه باسم المفصلة والمغيرة
He said: 'And among them is the generative power.' It is that which obtains from food what is suitable to become a principle of another individual of the same species as the eater and separates it into different parts, and imparts to it the dispositions by which it becomes similar in actuality. The majority hold that the generative power is that which obtains the matter and separates it, while the imparting of dispositions is another power called the form-giving power. It is a power whose function is to obtain the seed and separate it into different parts and suitable dispositions, by extracting from food after complete digestion to become a principle of another individual of the same species of the eater or of its genus, then it separates what is in it of the qualitative mixtures, mixing them according to each organ, then after transformations it imparts the forms, powers, and accidents that belong to the species from which it separated at the time of seeding or to its genus, as in the mule. The investigators hold that these actions are attributed to three powers: the first – that which draws blood to the testicles and acts upon it until it becomes semen, and it does not leave the testicles, and is specifically called the 'obtaining' power; the second – that which acts upon the semen, separating its qualitative mixtures and mixing them according to each organ, specifying for the nerve a particular temperament, for the artery a particular temperament, and for the bone a particular temperament. In short, it prepares the materials of the organs, and this is specifically called the 'separating' or 'changing' power.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage defines the generative power (القوة المولدة) and presents two scholarly views on its nature. First, it gives a functional definition: it obtains from food what can become the starting point (مبدأ) of another individual of the same species, separates it into different parts, and gives it the dispositions to become like the parent in actuality. Then it presents the view of the majority (الجمهور): the generative power only obtains the matter and separates it, while a separate power called the form-giving power (صورة) imparts the dispositions. The text elaborates that this form-giving power obtains the seed (بذر), separates it into parts and dispositions by extracting from food after digestive stages. Then it mentions the view of the investigators (المحققون), who say these actions are attributed to three distinct powers: (1) the 'obtaining' power (المحصلة) that draws blood to the testicles and transforms it into semen, (2) the 'separating' or 'changing' power (المفصلة or المغيرة) that acts on the semen to separate qualitative mixtures and assign specific temperaments to each organ, and (3) implicitly a third power that imparts forms after transformations.

Key Terms
مولدة
قوة مولدة
مادة
صورة
بذر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the generative power alone is responsible for both matter and form of the embryo.
Correction: The majority clearly distinguish between the generative power (responsible for obtaining and separating matter) and the form-giving power (responsible for imparting dispositions).

Checkpoint
According to the majority view, what does the generative power do?
A) It obtains matter and gives it form.
B) It only obtains matter and separates it.
C) It imparts dispositions to the embryo.
D) It digests food.

Key Takeaway
The generative power prepares the raw material; the form-giving power shapes it into a living being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the generative power only prepares raw material and another power shapes it, what does that teach you about your own tendency to focus on outward preparation while neglecting the deeper formation of your soul?
?
The form-giving power imparts specific dispositions to each organ. In your spiritual life, who or what is shaping your inner dispositions? Are you allowing the right 'form-giving' to transform you, or are you merely collecting raw experiences?
?
The passage describes a process from food to a fully formed being. Reflect: What are you 'digesting' daily that becomes the raw material for your character? If the seed of your actions is flawed, what hope for a good outcome? What will you change today?

The Investigators on Three Powers
Investigators' view on three powers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the investigators' position that the actions attributed to the generative power are actually performed by three distinct powers: the obtaining power, the separating/changing power, and a third implied power.

Core Idea
The investigators refine the generative power into three specialized powers.

The investigators offer a more detailed analysis, breaking down the generative functions into three separate powers. In this passage, they describe the first two: the power that draws blood to the testicles and forms semen, and the power that acts on the semen to assign specific temperaments to each organ.

Guided Path
1
Understand the investigators' starting point
The investigators hold that the actions of obtaining and forming the embryo are attributed to three powers, not just one or two.
They base their view on observations in humans and many animals.
2
Study the first power: the obtaining power
The first power draws blood to the testicles and transforms it into semen. It remains in the testicles and is called the 'obtaining power' (المحصلة).
3
Study the second power: the separating/changing power
The second power acts on the semen, separating its qualitative mixtures and mixing them to give each organ its specific temperament. It is called the 'separating' or 'changing' power (المفصلة or المغيرة).
This power prepares the materials for each organ.
4
Note the implied third power
Although the text mentions three powers, it only describes two explicitly. The third is likely the form-giving power that imparts final forms after the materials are prepared.
This connects back to the majority view's form-giving power.

The Objection and Its Refutation
Objection-and-answer regarding the formative faculty's precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that the formative faculty precedes the soul does not apply if the formative faculty belongs to the mother's rational soul or the child's plant soul.

Core Idea
The objection misfires when the formative faculty is not from the child's rational soul.

The philosophers claim that the soul's faculties are its tools, yet they say the formative faculty acts before the soul appears. This seems contradictory. Here, the author presents this objection and provides a nuanced answer: the contradiction only holds if the formative faculty belongs to the child's rational soul. If it belongs to the mother's soul or the child's plant soul, no problem arises.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objection: if the soul's faculties are its tools, they cannot act before the soul exists. The philosophers say the formative faculty acts before the rational soul.
The objection is based on the principle that a tool cannot precede its user.
2
Identify the answer's key distinction
The answer concedes the soul is not eternal but distinguishes whose soul the formative faculty belongs to. It only applies if it's from the child's rational soul.
The answer rules out two other possibilities: the child's plant soul or the mother's rational soul.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters for the coherence of the philosophers' position. The objection is not fatal; it only targets one specific interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما الاعتراض) بأن قوى النفس آلات لها وخوادم فيمتنع حدوثها قبل النفس، وفعلها بذاتها فإنما يتوجه لو جعل النفس حادثة بعد البدن والمصورة عن قوى نفس المولد، كالغاذية والنامية. ودل على اضطرابهم في ذلك، اضطرابهم في أن الجامع للأجزاء، والحافظ لها ماذا ؟ فذكر الإمام أن الجامع لأجزاء بدن الجنين نفس الأبوين، ثم يبقى المزاج في تدبير نفس الأم إلى أن يستعد كحدوث نفس تكون هي الحافظة له والجامعة لسائر الأجزاء. ونقل ابن سينا أن الجامع نفس الأبوين، والحافظ للاجتماع أولاً القوة المصورة لذلك البدن، ثم نفسه الناطقة . وصرح في الشفاء بأن الجامع للاسطقسات بدن كل حيوان والمؤلف لها على ما يصلح والحافظ لنظامه على ما ينبغي هي النفس التي له، والأشبه ما قيل، أن المتصرف أولاً نفس الأبوين بقواها إلى أن يفرز من الأخلاط، ما يصلح مادة للمنى، وبعدها لصورة تحفظ مزاجه، ثم يتكامل في الرحم إلى أن يستعد لنفس يصدر عنها، مع حفظ المزاج الأفعال النباتية، فتجذب الغذاء إلى تلك المادة، وتعدها لقبول نفس يصدر عنها مع ما سبق الأفعال الحيوانية، وهكذا إلى الناطقة. يورد ههنا سؤال. وهو أن الفلاسفة يجعلون المولدة والمصورة وغيرهما، قد قوى للنفس وآلات لها والنفس حادثة بعد حدوث المزاج، وتمام صور الأعضاء فالقول باستناد صور الأعضاء إلى المصورة، قول بحدوث الآلة قبل ذي الالة ، وفعلها بنفسها من غير مستعمل إياها وهو باطل. وجوابه بعد تسليم أن النفس ليست بقديمة، كما هو رأي بعض الفلاسفة، ولا حادثة قبل حدوث البدن، كما هو رأي بعض المليين أن ذلك إنما يرد لو جعلت المصورة من قوى النفس الناطقة للمولود، وأما لو جعلت من قوى نفسه النباتية ، المغايرة بالذات لنفسه الناطقة كما هو رأى البعض، أو من قوى النفس الناطقة للأم فلا إشكال إلا أن كلامهم مضطرب في ذلك، على ما يشعر به اضطرابهم في أن الجامع لأجزاء البدن ، هل هو الحافظ لها أم لا ، وفي أنه نفس المولود أم غيرها، فذكر الإمام أن الجامع لأجزاء النطفة نفس الوالدين، ثم أنه يبقى ذلك المزاج في تدبير نفس الأم إلى أن يستعد لقبول نفس، ثم إنها تصير بعد حدوثها، حافظة له وجامعة لسائر الأجزاء بطريق إيراد الغذاء الاستعدادات ونقل عن ابن سينا أن الجامع لأجزاء بدن الجنين نفس الوالدين، والحافظ لذلك الاجتماع أولاً القوة المصورة لذلك البدن ، ثم نفسه الناطقة، وتلك القوة ليست واحدة في جميع الأحوال، بل هي قوى متعاقبة بحسب المختلفة لمادة الجنين . وذكر في الشفاء أن النفس التي لكل حيوان هي جامعة إسطقسات بدنه، ومؤلفتها ومركبتها على نحو يصلح معه ان يكون بدناً لها، وهي حافظة لهذا البدن على النظام الذي ينبغي والأشبه بمقتضى قواعدهم ما ذكر في شرح الإشارات، وهي أن نفس الأبوين تجمع بالقوة الجاذبة، أجزاء غذائية ، ثم تجعلها أخلالها، وتفرد منها بالقوة المولدة مادة المنى، وتجعلها مستعدة لقبول قوة من شأنها إعداد المادة الصيرورتها إنساناً ، فتصير بتلك القوة منياً! وتلك القوة تكون صورة حافظة لمزاج المنى كالصورة المعدنية، ثم إن المنى يتزايد كمالا في
He said: (As for the objection) that the faculties of the soul are its tools and servants, so it is impossible for them to occur before the soul, and their acting by themselves — that only applies if the soul is made to occur after the body and the formative faculty is from the faculties of the generative soul, like the nutritive and growth faculties. And it indicates their confusion in that, their confusion about what is the gatherer of the parts and the preserver of them? So al-Imam mentioned that the gatherer of the parts of the fetus's body is the soul of the parents, then the temperament remains under the management of the mother's soul until it becomes ready for the occurrence of a soul that becomes the preserver of it and the gatherer of the rest of the parts. And Ibn Sina reported that the gatherer is the soul of the parents, and the first preserver of the union is the formative power of that body, then its rational soul. And he stated in al-Shifa' that the gatherer of the elements of every animal's body and the composer of them in what is suitable and the preserver of its order as is proper is the soul that belongs to it. And the more likely is what was said: that the first disposer is the soul of the parents with its powers until there is separated from the humors what is suitable as matter for the semen, and after it a form that preserves its temperament, then it develops in the womb until it becomes ready for a soul from which [actions] emanate, along with preserving the temperament for the plant actions, so it attracts nourishment to that matter and prepares it for the acceptance of a soul from which emanates, along with what preceded, the animal actions, and so on up to the rational [soul]. A question is raised here: namely, the philosophers make the generative, formative, and other powers as faculties of the soul and tools for it, while the soul occurs after the occurrence of the temperament and the completion of the forms of the limbs. So the statement that the forms of the limbs are based on the formative power is a statement of the occurrence of the tool before the one who uses the tool, and its acting by itself without an user of it, and that is false. And its answer, after conceding that the soul is not eternal, as is the view of some philosophers, nor does it occur before the body, as is the view of some religionists, is that this only applies if the formative power is made from the faculties of the rational soul of the child. But if it is made from the faculties of its plant soul, which is different in essence from its rational soul, as is the view of some, or from the faculties of the rational soul of the mother, then there is no problem. However, their statements are confused about this, as is indicated by their confusion about whether the gatherer of the parts of the body is the preserver of them or not, and whether it is the soul of the child or something else. So al-Imam mentioned that the gatherer of the parts of the sperm is the soul of the parents, then that temperament remains under the management of the mother's soul until it becomes ready for the acceptance of a soul, then it becomes after its occurrence a preserver of it and a gatherer of the rest of the parts by way of bringing nourishment [and] preparations. And he reported from Ibn Sina that the gatherer of the parts of the fetus's body is the soul of the parents, and the first preserver of that union is the formative power of that body, then its rational soul, and that power is not one in all states, but rather it is successive powers according to the different preparations of the matter of the fetus. And he mentioned in al-Shifa' that the soul that belongs to every animal is the gatherer of the elements of its body, and the composer and arranger of them in a manner that makes them suitable to be a body for it, and it is the preserver of this body in the proper order. And the more likely according to their principles is what was mentioned in Sharh al-Isharat: that the soul of the parents gathers, by the attractive power, nutritive parts, then makes them humors, and separates from them by the generative power the matter of the semen, and makes it ready to accept a power whose function is to prepare the matter for its becoming a human; thus it becomes by that power semen, and that power is a form that preserves the temperament of the semen like a mineral form, then the semen increases in perfection in
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the philosophers' view that the formative faculty (al-quwwa al-musawwira) is a tool of the soul, then provides an answer by distinguishing between different accounts of the soul's origin. The objection states that if the soul's faculties are its tools and servants, they cannot exist before the soul itself; yet the philosophers claim the formative faculty acts before the rational soul appears. The answer shows that this objection only applies if the formative faculty belongs to the child's rational soul. However, if it belongs to the child's plant soul (different in essence from its rational soul) or to the mother's rational soul, then there is no problem. The passage then notes that the philosophers' own statements are confused on this point, as shown by their disagreement on what gathers and preserves the body's parts. It cites al-Imam (likely al-Ghazali) and Ibn Sina, showing that they give different accounts: al-Imam says the gatherer is the soul of the parents, while Ibn Sina says the gatherer is the parents' soul but the preserver is first the formative power, then the rational soul. The passage also mentions a view from Sharh al-Isharat describing a succession of powers leading from the parents' soul to the semen's preservation. This confusion among the philosophers serves to undermine the force of the original objection.

Key Terms
النفس
القوى
المصورة
المولدة
الناطقة
النباتية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a fatal refutation of all philosophers' views on the soul.
Correction: The objection only applies if the formative faculty is from the child's rational soul; other interpretations escape it.

Checkpoint
Under which condition does the objection against the philosophers hold according to the answer?
A) If the formative faculty belongs to the mother's rational soul.
B) If the formative faculty belongs to the child's plant soul.
C) If the formative faculty belongs to the child's rational soul.
D) If the soul is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The objection that the formative faculty precedes the soul is only a problem if the formative faculty belongs to the child's rational soul; other origins avoid the difficulty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever rejected a whole argument because you assumed it applied universally, when in fact it only applies under specific conditions? Where in your own thinking are you making that mistake right now? Will you re-examine those assumptions today?
?
The objection relies on the idea that a tool cannot act without its user. In your spiritual life, are you acting as a 'tool' without the 'user'—doing religious deeds without conscious connection to the One who should be using them? What would it mean to let God be the user of your actions?
?
The author concedes part of the objection before refuting it. Is there a criticism of your faith or practice that you have been dismissing entirely, when you should first admit its validity in some cases? Can you humbly accept the part that is true and then clarify the rest without defensiveness?

The Philosophers' Confusion on the Gatherer and Preserver
Enumeration of philosophers' conflicting views on the gatherer and preserver of the body's parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various conflicting views among philosophers (al-Imam, Ibn Sina, others) on what gathers and preserves the parts of the body during fetal development.

Core Idea
Philosophers disagree on the identity of the gatherer and preserver, undermining their consistency.

The author notes that the philosophers are confused about a fundamental question: what gathers the parts of the body and preserves them? This confusion is used to weaken the force of the objection about the formative faculty. By showing that even the philosophers cannot agree, the author argues that their position is not coherent. The passage presents several competing accounts from al-Imam, Ibn Sina, and others.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the different views
Identify the three or more views mentioned: al-Imam's view, Ibn Sina's view, the view from al-Shifa', and the preferred view from Sharh al-Isharat.
Each view assigns the role of gatherer/preserver to a different entity.
2
Compare the views
Note how they differ: al-Imam says the gatherer is the parents' soul then the child's soul; Ibn Sina says the preserver is first the formative power then the rational soul; etc.
The lack of consensus shows confusion.
3
Relate to the larger argument
Understand why this confusion matters: it shows that the philosophers' own theories are inconsistent, so their objection against the theologians is weakened.
The confusion is presented as evidence of the philosophers' inconsistency.

He said: (As for the objection) that the faculties of the soul are its tools and servants, so it is impossible for them to occur before the soul, and their acting by themselves — that only applies if the soul is made to occur after the body and the formative faculty is from the faculties of the generative soul, like the nutritive and growth faculties. And it indicates their confusion in that, their confusion about what is the gatherer of the parts and the preserver of them? So al-Imam mentioned that the gatherer of the parts of the fetus's body is the soul of the parents, then the temperament remains under the management of the mother's soul until it becomes ready for the occurrence of a soul that becomes the preserver of it and the gatherer of the rest of the parts. And Ibn Sina reported that the gatherer is the soul of the parents, and the first preserver of the union is the formative power of that body, then its rational soul. And he stated in al-Shifa' that the gatherer of the elements of every animal's body and the composer of them in what is suitable and the preserver of its order as is proper is the soul that belongs to it. And the more likely is what was said: that the first disposer is the soul of the parents with its powers until there is separated from the humors what is suitable as matter for the semen, and after it a form that preserves its temperament, then it develops in the womb until it becomes ready for a soul from which [actions] emanate, along with preserving the temperament for the plant actions, so it attracts nourishment to that matter and prepares it for the acceptance of a soul from which emanates, along with what preceded, the animal actions, and so on up to the rational [soul]. A question is raised here: namely, the philosophers make the generative, formative, and other powers as faculties of the soul and tools for it, while the soul occurs after the occurrence of the temperament and the completion of the forms of the limbs. So the statement that the forms of the limbs are based on the formative power is a statement of the occurrence of the tool before the one who uses the tool, and its acting by itself without an user of it, and that is false. And its answer, after conceding that the soul is not eternal, as is the view of some philosophers, nor does it occur before the body, as is the view of some religionists, is that this only applies if the formative power is made from the faculties of the rational soul of the child. But if it is made from the faculties of its plant soul, which is different in essence from its rational soul, as is the view of some, or from the faculties of the rational soul of the mother, then there is no problem. However, their statements are confused about this, as is indicated by their confusion about whether the gatherer of the parts of the body is the preserver of them or not, and whether it is the soul of the child or something else. So al-Imam mentioned that the gatherer of the parts of the sperm is the soul of the parents, then that temperament remains under the management of the mother's soul until it becomes ready for the acceptance of a soul, then it becomes after its occurrence a preserver of it and a gatherer of the rest of the parts by way of bringing nourishment [and] preparations. And he reported from Ibn Sina that the gatherer of the parts of the fetus's body is the soul of the parents, and the first preserver of that union is the formative power of that body, then its rational soul, and that power is not one in all states, but rather it is successive powers according to the different preparations of the matter of the fetus. And he mentioned in al-Shifa' that the soul that belongs to every animal is the gatherer of the elements of its body, and the composer and arranger of them in a manner that makes them suitable to be a body for it, and it is the preserver of this body in the proper order. And the more likely according to their principles is what was mentioned in Sharh al-Isharat: that the soul of the parents gathers, by the attractive power, nutritive parts, then makes them humors, and separates from them by the generative power the matter of the semen, and makes it ready to accept a power whose function is to prepare the matter for its becoming a human; thus it becomes by that power semen, and that power is a form that preserves the temperament of the semen like a mineral form, then the semen increases in perfection in

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the philosophers' view that the formative faculty (al-quwwa al-musawwira) is a tool of the soul, then provides an answer by distinguishing between different accounts of the soul's origin. The objection states that if the soul's faculties are its tools and servants, they cannot exist before the soul itself; yet the philosophers claim the formative faculty acts before the rational soul appears. The answer shows that this objection only applies if the formative faculty belongs to the child's rational soul. However, if it belongs to the child's plant soul (different in essence from its rational soul) or to the mother's rational soul, then there is no problem. The passage then notes that the philosophers' own statements are confused on this point, as shown by their disagreement on what gathers and preserves the body's parts. It cites al-Imam (likely al-Ghazali) and Ibn Sina, showing that they give different accounts: al-Imam says the gatherer is the soul of the parents, while Ibn Sina says the gatherer is the parents' soul but the preserver is first the formative power, then the rational soul. The passage also mentions a view from Sharh al-Isharat describing a succession of powers leading from the parents' soul to the semen's preservation. This confusion among the philosophers serves to undermine the force of the original objection.

Initial Views on Collector and Preserver
States the confusion and presents two initial views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the confusion among scholars about whether the collector and preserver of body parts are the same entity, and the early views of the Imam and Ibn Sina on this topic.

Core Idea
The collector may not be the preserver, and opinions differ on which soul is involved.

The passage opens by highlighting a fundamental confusion: is the one who collects the parts of the body also the one who preserves them? And is this agent the soul of the newborn or something else? The Imam and Ibn Sina offer contrasting answers that set the stage for a more detailed analysis later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the confusion stated at the beginning and the two views presented.
Notice the words 'جَامع' and 'حَافِظ'—they are key.
2
Identify the confusion
The author notes that scholars are confused about whether the collector and preserver are the same, and whether the agent is the newborn's soul or something else. Underline this in your mind.
3
Compare the views of Imam and Ibn Sina
The Imam holds that the collector is the parents' soul, and the preserver becomes the newborn's soul after its occurrence. Ibn Sina says the initial preserver is the formative power, then the rational soul.
Ibn Sina also mentions that the powers are successive, not one.
4
Reflect on the source of confusion
Ask yourself why this confusion matters for understanding how the body is formed and preserved. This connects to the larger debate on the soul's role.

حادثة قبل حدوث البدن، كما هو رأي بعض المليين أن ذلك إنما يرد لو جعلت المصورة من قوى النفس الناطقة للمولود، وأما لو جعلت من قوى نفسه النباتية، المغايرة بالذات لنفسه الناطقة كما هو رأى البعض، أو من قوى النفس الناطقة للأم فلا إشكال إلا أن كلامهم مضطرب في ذلك، على ما يشعر به اضطرابهم في أن الجامع لأجزاء البدن، هل هو الحافظ لها أم لا، وفي أنه نفس المولود أم غيرها، فذكر الإمام أن الجامع لأجزاء النطفة نفس الوالدين، ثم أنه يبقى ذلك المزاج في تدبير نفس الأم إلى أن يستعد لقبول نفس، ثم إنها تصير بعد حدوثها، حافظة له وجامعة لسائر الأجزاء بطريق إيراد الغذاء. ونقل عن ابن سينا أن الجامع لأجزاء بدن الجنين نفس الوالدين، والحافظ لذلك الاجتماع أولاً القوة المصورة لذلك البدن، ثم نفسه الناطقة، وتلك القوة ليست واحدة في جميع الأحوال، بل هي قوى متعاقبة بحسب المختلفة لمادة الجنين. وذكر في الشفاء أن النفس التي لكل حيوان هي جامعة إسطقسات بدنه، ومؤلفتها ومركبتها على نحو يصلح معه ان يكون بدناً لها، وهي حافظة لهذا البدن على النظام الذي ينبغي. والأشبه بمقتضى قواعدهم ما ذكر في شرح الإشارات، وهي أن نفس الأبوين تجمع بالقوة الجاذبة، أجزاء غذائية، ثم تجعلها أخلالها، وتفرد منها بالقوة المولدة مادة المنى، وتجعلها مستعدة لقبول قوة من شأنها إعداد المادة الصيرورتها إنساناً، فتصير بتلك القوة منياً! وتلك القوة تكون صورة حافظة لمزاج المنى كالصورة المعدنية، ثم إن المنى يتزايد كمالا في الرحم بحسب استعدادات يكتسبها هناك إلى أن يصير مستعداً لقبول نفس أكمل، يصدر عنها مع حفظ المادة، الأفعال النباتية فتجذب الغذاء، ويضيفها إلى تلك المادة فيتمها، وتتكامل المادة بترتيبها إياها، فتصير تلك الصورة مصدراً مع ما كان يصدر عنها بهذه الأفاعيل. وهكذا إلى أن تصير مستعدة لقبول نفس أكمل يصدر عنها، مع جميع ما تقدم الأفعال الحيوانية أيضاً، فيصدر عنها تلك الأفعال أيضاً، فيتم البدن ويتكامل إلى يصير مستعداً لقبول نفس ناطقة يصدر عنها، مع جميع ما تقدم النطق، وتبقى مدبرة في البدن إلى أن يحل الأجل. وقد شبهوا تلك القوى في أحوالها من مبدأ حدوثها إلى استكمالها نفساً مجردة بحرارة تحدث في فحم من نار مشتعلة مجاورة، ثم تشتد، فإن الفحم بتلك الحرارة يستعد لأن يتجمر، وبالتجمر يستعد، لأن يشتعل ناراً، شبيهة بالنار المجاورة، فمبدأ الحرارة الجاذبة في الفحم، كتلك الصورة الحافظة واشتدادها، كمبدأ الأفعال النباتية وتجمرها، كمبدأ الأفعال الحيوانية، واشتعالها ناراً كالناطقة، وظاهر أن كل ما يتأخر يصدر عنه مثل ما صدر عن المتقدم وزيادة، فجميع هذه القوى كشيء واحد متوجه من حد ما من النقصان إلى حد ما من الكمال، واسم النفس واقع منها على الثلاث الأخيرة، فهي على اختلاف مراتبها نفس البدن المولود وتبين من ذلك أن الجامع للأجزاء الغذائية الواقعة في المنيين هو نفس الأبوين، وهو غير حافظها، والجامع للأجزاء المضافة إليها، إلى أن يتم البدن، وإلى آخر العمر، والحافظ للمزاج هو نفس المولود.
The incident before the occurrence of the body, as is the view of some of the Millis, that this only arises if the formative power is made one of the powers of the rational soul of the newborn. But if it is made one of the powers of his vegetative soul, different in essence from his rational soul as is the view of some, or from the powers of the mother's rational soul, then there is no problem; except that their speech is confused about that, as indicated by their confusion about whether the collector of the parts of the body is the preserver of them or not, and whether it is the soul of the newborn or something else. So the Imam mentioned that the collector of the parts of the semen is the soul of the parents; then that mixture remains under the management of the mother's soul until it becomes ready to accept a soul; then after its occurrence it becomes its preserver and collector of the rest of the parts by way of bringing nourishment. And it is transmitted from Ibn Sina that the collector of the parts of the embryo's body is the soul of the parents, and the preserver of that combination is initially the formative power of that body, then its rational soul, and that power is not one in all states, but rather successive powers according to the different [states] of the embryo's matter. And he mentioned in al-Shifa' that the soul of every animal is the collector of the elements of its body, their composer and combiner in a manner suitable for it to be a body for it, and it is the preserver of this body in the proper order. And the more similar to the implication of their principles is what was mentioned in Sharh al-Isharat, which is that the soul of the parents gathers, by the attractive power, nutritive parts, then makes them into its humors, and separates from them by the generative power the substance of the semen, and makes it ready to accept a power whose function is to prepare the matter for becoming a human, so it becomes by that power semen! And that power becomes a form preserving the temperament of the semen like the mineral form. Then the semen increases in perfection in the womb according to preparations it acquires there until it becomes ready to accept a more complete soul, from which emanate, with preservation of the matter, the vegetative acts: it attracts nourishment, and adds it to that matter, thereby completing it, and the matter becomes complete by its arrangement of it, so that form becomes a source, along with what previously emanated from it, of these actions. And so on until it becomes ready to accept a more complete soul from which emanate, along with all that preceded, the animal acts as well; so those acts also emanate from it, and the body becomes complete and perfect until it becomes ready to accept a rational soul from which emanate, along with all that preceded, speech, and it remains managing the body until the appointed term arrives. And they compared those powers in their states from the beginning of their occurrence to their completion as an abstract soul to heat that occurs in charcoal from an adjacent blazing fire, then it intensifies: for the charcoal by that heat becomes ready to ignite, and by ignition becomes ready to flame into a fire similar to the adjacent fire. So the beginning of the attractive heat in the charcoal is like that preserving form, and its intensification is like the beginning of vegetative acts, and its ignition is like the beginning of animal acts, and its flaming into fire is like the rational soul. It is apparent that each later thing emanates from it the like of what emanated from the earlier and more, so all these powers are like one thing moving from a certain limit of deficiency to a certain limit of perfection, and the name 'soul' applies to the last three of them; they are, according to their different ranks, the soul of the newborn body. And it becomes clear from that that the collector of the nutritive parts occurring in the two parents is the soul of the parents, and it is not their preserver, and the collector of the parts added to it until the body is completed and until the end of life, and the preserver of the temperament, is the soul of the newborn.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a comparative exposition of different philosophical views on the formation and preservation of the human body, particularly regarding the powers (quwwa) that collect and preserve the body's parts. It begins by considering an objection concerning the formative power (al-quwwa al-muṣawwira) and whether it belongs to the rational soul of the newborn, the vegetative soul, or the mother's rational soul. The author notes confusion among thinkers about whether the collector (jāmiʿ) of body parts is also the preserver (ḥāfiẓ), and whether it is the newborn's soul or something else. The 'Imam' (likely al-Ghazali or Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) holds that the collector of the semen's parts is the soul of the parents; the mixture then remains under the mother's soul's management until it becomes ready for a soul, which then becomes its preserver and collector. The passage then summarizes Ibn Sina's view: the collector is the parents' soul, but the preserver is initially the formative power, then the rational soul; these are not one but successive powers. In al-Shifa', Ibn Sina states that the soul of every animal collects, composes, and combines the elements of its body and preserves it properly. The view most consistent with their principles is from Sharh al-Isharat: the parents' soul gathers nutritive parts via the attractive power, separates the semen via the generative power, which becomes ready for a power that prepares it to become human; this power is a form preserving the semen's temperament like a mineral form. The semen then increases in perfection, acquiring preparations for a more complete soul that performs vegetative acts, then animal acts, then rational speech. The author uses a striking analogy: these powers are like heat in charcoal from an adjacent fire, intensifying from attractive heat to vegetative acts, animal acts, and finally rational fire. Each later stage includes the earlier and adds more, so all powers are like one thing moving from deficiency to perfection. The name 'soul' applies to the last three (vegetative, animal, rational), which are different ranks of the soul of the newborn. It is concluded that the collector of nutritive parts in the two parents is the parents' soul (not the preserver), while the collector of additional parts throughout life and the preserver of temperament is the newborn's soul.

The Process of Successive Powers and the Charcoal Analogy
Presents the detailed account from Sharh al-Isharat with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed process of body formation from the parents' soul to the rational soul, as described in Sharh al-Isharat, and the analogy of charcoal fire that illustrates the successive powers.

Core Idea
The soul develops through successive powers, like heat in charcoal intensifying to flame.

After the initial views, the text presents the account from Sharh al-Isharat as the most consistent with their philosophical principles. This account traces a stepwise progression from the parents' gathering of nutritive parts to the emergence of vegetative, animal, and finally rational powers. The process is then illuminated by a striking analogy: heat in a charcoal fire, where each stage prepares for the next.

Guided Path
1
Read the detailed process from Sharh al-Isharat
Read the passage from 'والأشبه بمقتضى قواعدهم' to the end. Follow the sequence: parents' soul gathers, forms semen, preservative form, vegetative acts, animal acts, rational soul.
Notice the word 'اِسْتِعْدَاد' (preparedness) appears repeatedly.
2
Examine the analogy step by step
The analogy compares the powers to heat in charcoal: attractive heat → intensification (vegetative) → ignition (animal) → flame (rational). Map each stage to the corresponding power.
The analogy shows that later stages include and add to the earlier ones.
3
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that the soul of the parents is only the collector of nutritive parts, not the preserver; the preserver and the collector of parts throughout life is the newborn's soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حادثة قبل حدوث البدن، كما هو رأي بعض المليين أن ذلك إنما يرد لو جعلت المصورة من قوى النفس الناطقة للمولود، وأما لو جعلت من قوى نفسه النباتية، المغايرة بالذات لنفسه الناطقة كما هو رأى البعض، أو من قوى النفس الناطقة للأم فلا إشكال إلا أن كلامهم مضطرب في ذلك، على ما يشعر به اضطرابهم في أن الجامع لأجزاء البدن، هل هو الحافظ لها أم لا، وفي أنه نفس المولود أم غيرها، فذكر الإمام أن الجامع لأجزاء النطفة نفس الوالدين، ثم أنه يبقى ذلك المزاج في تدبير نفس الأم إلى أن يستعد لقبول نفس، ثم إنها تصير بعد حدوثها، حافظة له وجامعة لسائر الأجزاء بطريق إيراد الغذاء. ونقل عن ابن سينا أن الجامع لأجزاء بدن الجنين نفس الوالدين، والحافظ لذلك الاجتماع أولاً القوة المصورة لذلك البدن، ثم نفسه الناطقة، وتلك القوة ليست واحدة في جميع الأحوال، بل هي قوى متعاقبة بحسب المختلفة لمادة الجنين. وذكر في الشفاء أن النفس التي لكل حيوان هي جامعة إسطقسات بدنه، ومؤلفتها ومركبتها على نحو يصلح معه ان يكون بدناً لها، وهي حافظة لهذا البدن على النظام الذي ينبغي. والأشبه بمقتضى قواعدهم ما ذكر في شرح الإشارات، وهي أن نفس الأبوين تجمع بالقوة الجاذبة، أجزاء غذائية، ثم تجعلها أخلالها، وتفرد منها بالقوة المولدة مادة المنى، وتجعلها مستعدة لقبول قوة من شأنها إعداد المادة الصيرورتها إنساناً، فتصير بتلك القوة منياً! وتلك القوة تكون صورة حافظة لمزاج المنى كالصورة المعدنية، ثم إن المنى يتزايد كمالا في الرحم بحسب استعدادات يكتسبها هناك إلى أن يصير مستعداً لقبول نفس أكمل، يصدر عنها مع حفظ المادة، الأفعال النباتية فتجذب الغذاء، ويضيفها إلى تلك المادة فيتمها، وتتكامل المادة بترتيبها إياها، فتصير تلك الصورة مصدراً مع ما كان يصدر عنها بهذه الأفاعيل. وهكذا إلى أن تصير مستعدة لقبول نفس أكمل يصدر عنها، مع جميع ما تقدم الأفعال الحيوانية أيضاً، فيصدر عنها تلك الأفعال أيضاً، فيتم البدن ويتكامل إلى يصير مستعداً لقبول نفس ناطقة يصدر عنها، مع جميع ما تقدم النطق، وتبقى مدبرة في البدن إلى أن يحل الأجل. وقد شبهوا تلك القوى في أحوالها من مبدأ حدوثها إلى استكمالها نفساً مجردة بحرارة تحدث في فحم من نار مشتعلة مجاورة، ثم تشتد، فإن الفحم بتلك الحرارة يستعد لأن يتجمر، وبالتجمر يستعد، لأن يشتعل ناراً، شبيهة بالنار المجاورة، فمبدأ الحرارة الجاذبة في الفحم، كتلك الصورة الحافظة واشتدادها، كمبدأ الأفعال النباتية وتجمرها، كمبدأ الأفعال الحيوانية، واشتعالها ناراً كالناطقة، وظاهر أن كل ما يتأخر يصدر عنه مثل ما صدر عن المتقدم وزيادة، فجميع هذه القوى كشيء واحد متوجه من حد ما من النقصان إلى حد ما من الكمال، واسم النفس واقع منها على الثلاث الأخيرة، فهي على اختلاف مراتبها نفس البدن المولود وتبين من ذلك أن الجامع للأجزاء الغذائية الواقعة في المنيين هو نفس الأبوين، وهو غير حافظها، والجامع للأجزاء المضافة إليها، إلى أن يتم البدن، وإلى آخر العمر، والحافظ للمزاج هو نفس المولود.
The incident before the occurrence of the body, as is the view of some of the Millis, that this only arises if the formative power is made one of the powers of the rational soul of the newborn. But if it is made one of the powers of his vegetative soul, different in essence from his rational soul as is the view of some, or from the powers of the mother's rational soul, then there is no problem; except that their speech is confused about that, as indicated by their confusion about whether the collector of the parts of the body is the preserver of them or not, and whether it is the soul of the newborn or something else. So the Imam mentioned that the collector of the parts of the semen is the soul of the parents; then that mixture remains under the management of the mother's soul until it becomes ready to accept a soul; then after its occurrence it becomes its preserver and collector of the rest of the parts by way of bringing nourishment. And it is transmitted from Ibn Sina that the collector of the parts of the embryo's body is the soul of the parents, and the preserver of that combination is initially the formative power of that body, then its rational soul, and that power is not one in all states, but rather successive powers according to the different [states] of the embryo's matter. And he mentioned in al-Shifa' that the soul of every animal is the collector of the elements of its body, their composer and combiner in a manner suitable for it to be a body for it, and it is the preserver of this body in the proper order. And the more similar to the implication of their principles is what was mentioned in Sharh al-Isharat, which is that the soul of the parents gathers, by the attractive power, nutritive parts, then makes them into its humors, and separates from them by the generative power the substance of the semen, and makes it ready to accept a power whose function is to prepare the matter for becoming a human, so it becomes by that power semen! And that power becomes a form preserving the temperament of the semen like the mineral form. Then the semen increases in perfection in the womb according to preparations it acquires there until it becomes ready to accept a more complete soul, from which emanate, with preservation of the matter, the vegetative acts: it attracts nourishment, and adds it to that matter, thereby completing it, and the matter becomes complete by its arrangement of it, so that form becomes a source, along with what previously emanated from it, of these actions. And so on until it becomes ready to accept a more complete soul from which emanate, along with all that preceded, the animal acts as well; so those acts also emanate from it, and the body becomes complete and perfect until it becomes ready to accept a rational soul from which emanate, along with all that preceded, speech, and it remains managing the body until the appointed term arrives. And they compared those powers in their states from the beginning of their occurrence to their completion as an abstract soul to heat that occurs in charcoal from an adjacent blazing fire, then it intensifies: for the charcoal by that heat becomes ready to ignite, and by ignition becomes ready to flame into a fire similar to the adjacent fire. So the beginning of the attractive heat in the charcoal is like that preserving form, and its intensification is like the beginning of vegetative acts, and its ignition is like the beginning of animal acts, and its flaming into fire is like the rational soul. It is apparent that each later thing emanates from it the like of what emanated from the earlier and more, so all these powers are like one thing moving from a certain limit of deficiency to a certain limit of perfection, and the name 'soul' applies to the last three of them; they are, according to their different ranks, the soul of the newborn body. And it becomes clear from that that the collector of the nutritive parts occurring in the two parents is the soul of the parents, and it is not their preserver, and the collector of the parts added to it until the body is completed and until the end of life, and the preserver of the temperament, is the soul of the newborn.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a comparative exposition of different philosophical views on the formation and preservation of the human body, particularly regarding the powers (quwwa) that collect and preserve the body's parts. It begins by considering an objection concerning the formative power (al-quwwa al-muṣawwira) and whether it belongs to the rational soul of the newborn, the vegetative soul, or the mother's rational soul. The author notes confusion among thinkers about whether the collector (jāmiʿ) of body parts is also the preserver (ḥāfiẓ), and whether it is the newborn's soul or something else. The 'Imam' (likely al-Ghazali or Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) holds that the collector of the semen's parts is the soul of the parents; the mixture then remains under the mother's soul's management until it becomes ready for a soul, which then becomes its preserver and collector. The passage then summarizes Ibn Sina's view: the collector is the parents' soul, but the preserver is initially the formative power, then the rational soul; these are not one but successive powers. In al-Shifa', Ibn Sina states that the soul of every animal collects, composes, and combines the elements of its body and preserves it properly. The view most consistent with their principles is from Sharh al-Isharat: the parents' soul gathers nutritive parts via the attractive power, separates the semen via the generative power, which becomes ready for a power that prepares it to become human; this power is a form preserving the semen's temperament like a mineral form. The semen then increases in perfection, acquiring preparations for a more complete soul that performs vegetative acts, then animal acts, then rational speech. The author uses a striking analogy: these powers are like heat in charcoal from an adjacent fire, intensifying from attractive heat to vegetative acts, animal acts, and finally rational fire. Each later stage includes the earlier and adds more, so all powers are like one thing moving from deficiency to perfection. The name 'soul' applies to the last three (vegetative, animal, rational), which are different ranks of the soul of the newborn. It is concluded that the collector of nutritive parts in the two parents is the parents' soul (not the preserver), while the collector of additional parts throughout life and the preserver of temperament is the newborn's soul.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the soul is a single unchanging entity from the beginning.
Correction: The passage shows that there are successive powers (preservative form, vegetative, animal, rational) that culminate in the rational soul, not a single static soul.

Checkpoint

A) The initial heat
B) The intensification of heat
C) The ignition
D) The flaming into fire

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul is the preserver of your body from birth to death, how does your daily neglect of your spiritual development dishonour that trust? What specific change will you make this week to actively nourish your soul's progression?
?

?
The rational soul is the ultimate stage, yet it remains managing the body until the end. Do you live as if your soul is merely managing your body for worldly ends, or as if it is preparing for a life beyond? Do the Powers of the Soul Have Multiple Essences?
Opens the discussion on multiplicity of powers in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the multiplicity of acts does not necessarily imply multiple essences, and how receptacles and instruments serve as key factors in the discussion.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of acts can be explained by instruments and receptacles, not by multiple essences.

This lesson examines a hesitation among scholars regarding whether the various powers of the soul are multiple in essence. The text notes that the diversity of acts, strengths, and temporal origins can be attributed simply to different instruments and receptive capacities. We will explore the opening argument and the sub-questions that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the hesitation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the phrase 'taraddud' (hesitation) which signals a point of scholarly disagreement.
The passage is incomplete; focus on the part we have.
2
Identify the key reasoning
The author says it is not impossible to attribute multiplicity of acts, their differences in strength/weakness, and their disparity in origination/remaining to differences in receptacles and instruments. Understand that this reasoning allows for a single essence to produce diverse effects.
This is the core argument against multiplicity of essences.
3
List the sub-questions raised
The passage branches into three sub-questions: (1) whether the powers are other than the vegetative or animal soul, (2) whether the nutritive power is other than the servant powers, and (3) an incomplete question about the principle. Identify these as further investigations of the same issue.
These sub-questions show the depth of the discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ثم لهم تردد في تعدد هذه القوى بالذات، إذ لا يمتنع استناد تعدد الأفعال واختلافها بالقوة والضعف وتفاوتها بالحدوث والبقاء، إلى اختلاف القوابل والآلات، ثم في مغايرتها للنفس النباتية أو الحيوانية، وفي مغايرة الغاذية للخوادم، وفي أن مبدأ
He said: Then they have hesitation about the multiplicity of these powers in essence, since it is not impossible to attribute the multiplicity of acts and their difference in strength and weakness and their disparity in origination and remaining to the difference of receptacles and instruments; then about their being other than the vegetative or animal soul, and about the nutritive power being other than the servant powers, and about whether the principle...
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage opens a new discussion on whether the successive powers described earlier (formative, vegetative, animal, rational) are multiple in essence (bi al-dhāt) or merely different manifestations of one essence. The author notes that there is hesitation (taraddud) among scholars on this point. The reason is that it is not impossible to explain the multiplicity of acts, their varying strengths, and their differences in origination and duration by reference to different receptacles (qawābil) and instruments (ālāt) rather than multiple distinct essences. The discussion then branches into further sub-questions: whether these powers are other than (i.e., distinct from) the vegetative or animal soul, and whether the nutritive power (al-ghādhiya) is other than the servant powers (al-khādim). The passage breaks off at 'and about whether the principle' (wa fī anna mabda'), leaving the thought incomplete. This indicates the beginning of a detailed investigation into the identity and distinction of the soul's faculties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel torn between a bodily urge and a spiritual yearning, do you experience them as two separate selves fighting inside you? This week, before acting on a strong desire, pause and ask: 'Is this really a different me, or just my one soul using a different tool?' Commit to that pause.
?
Think of a time when you attributed your actions to a 'bad side' or a 'good side' of yourself. Considering this lesson, could it be that you have one soul but the diversity of its acts comes from different receptacles and instruments? How does that shift your responsibility for your actions? Resolve to stop dividing yourself into pieces and own all your actions as expressions of your single soul.
?
The passage suggests that even the nutritive power might not be separate from the soul itself. Consider: if the most basic functions of your body are not foreign to your soul but are its own acts, what does that say about the sanctity of your physical existence? Will you treat your body and its needs with more reverence, knowing they come from the same soul that aspires to God?

Defining Life: Nutrition vs. Sensation
Presentation of the two definitions of life and their implications for plants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opposing definitions of life used in the debate about whether plants are alive.

Core Idea
Life can mean nutrition or sensation—two different starting points.

This lesson examines the core disagreement over plant life. Some scholars define life as the ability to grow and nourish, while others insist it requires sensation and voluntary movement. We'll see how these definitions lead to opposite conclusions about plants.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Start with the first lines: 'The plant, just as it is not an animal, is not alive...'. Notice that the initial statement takes one side.
The author first presents the view that denies plant life outright.
2
Identify the two definitions
Look for the two definitions of life: one as 'nutrition and growth', another as 'the source of sensation and voluntary movement'.
These are the opposing definitions that drive the dispute.
3
Contrast the rulings
See how each definition leads to a different verdict: the first makes plants alive, the second makes them not alive.
The passage explicitly states 'so it is said: it is alive' and 'it is said: no'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[قال (خاتمة)
النبات كما أنه ليس بحيوان، ليس بحي، لأن الحياة صفة تقتضي الحس، والحركة الإرادية، ومنهم من جعل التصرف في الغذاء حياة، فسماه حياً، ومنهم من بالغ فجعل للنبات مع الحس عقلاً].
لا خلاف في أن النبات ليس بحيوان لأن المراد به ما علم فيه تحقق الحس والحركة، وإنما الخلاف في حياته. فقيل هو حي، لأن الحياة صفة التغذية والتنمية . وقيل :لا : إذ الحياة صفة هي مبدأ الحس والحركة الإرادية . هي مبدأ واعترض بأنا لا نسلّم انتفاء ذلك في النبات غاية الأمر انتفاء العلم بتحققه فيه، ومنهم من ادعى تحققه فيه، متسشهداً بالأمارات على ما سبق، ومنهم من بلغ في اتصافه بالإدراك، حتى أثبت له إدراك الكليات وهو المعنى بالعقل، زعما منه، أن ما يشاهد من ميل إناث النخيل إلى بعض الذكور دون البعض، وميل عروقها إلى الصوب (۱) الذي فيه الماء، وانحرافها في صعودها عن الجدار المجاور، لا يتأتى بدون ذلك، وهذا ينسب إلى جمع من قدماء الحكماء.
[He said (Conclusion): The plant, just as it is not an animal, is not alive, because life is an attribute that necessitates sensation and voluntary movement. And among them is one who made the management of food life, so he called it alive. And among them is one who went so far as to attribute to the plant, along with sensation, intellect.] There is no disagreement that the plant is not an animal, because what is meant by animal is that in which sensation and movement are known to be realized. Rather the disagreement is concerning its life. So it is said: it is alive, because life is the attribute of nutrition and growth. And it is said: no, because life is an attribute that is the source of sensation and voluntary movement. It is the source. And it is objected: we do not concede the absence of that in the plant; at most it is the absence of knowledge of its realization in it. And among them is one who claimed its realization in it, adducing indications as mentioned earlier. And among them is one who went so far in attributing perception to it that he affirmed for it the perception of universals, which is the meaning of intellect, claiming that what is observed of the inclination of female date palms towards some males over others, and the inclination of their roots towards the rain (1) that contains water, and their deviation in climbing away from the adjacent wall, cannot occur without that. And this is attributed to a group of the ancient sages.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a concluding discussion on the status of plants. It begins by stating that plants are not animals and not alive if life is defined by sensation and voluntary movement. Some, however, define life as the ability to manage food, so they consider plants alive. Others go further, attributing both sensation and intellect to plants. The author then clarifies that there is no dispute that plants are not animals (since animals have sensation and voluntary movement), but the dispute is about whether they are alive. One opinion holds that life is the attribute of nutrition and growth, so plants are alive. Another opinion says life is the source of sensation and voluntary movement, so plants are not alive. An objection is raised: we do not concede that sensation and movement are absent in plants; we simply lack knowledge of their realization. Some have claimed that plants do have sensation and even intellect, using examples like female date palms turning toward specific male trees and roots turning toward water. They argue this could not occur without perception. This view is attributed to a group of ancient sages. The passage thus presents multiple views on plant life and perception, leaving the matter open.

الحركة الإرادية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All scholars agree that plants are not alive.
Correction: The passage shows a real disagreement: some define life as nutrition, making plants alive; others define it as sensation, making them not alive.

B) Life is sensation and voluntary movement

The Epistemic Objection: Absence of Proof is Not Proof of Absence
Presentation of an epistemological objection against denying plant life based on lack of evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an important epistemological argument: that lack of known evidence for plant sensation does not prove it doesn't exist.

Core Idea
We may not know if plants sense, but that doesn't mean they don't.

This lesson focuses on a critical objection raised in the debate: just because we haven't detected sensation in plants doesn't mean it is absent. This argument challenges the assumption that plants lack life because they lack apparent sensation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the objection
Find the line that begins with 'And it is objected'. This is the key epistemological point.
The objection directly challenges the reasoning that plants lack sensation.
2
Parse the objection's logic
The objection says: we do not concede that sensation and movement are absent; we only lack knowledge of their presence.
This distinguishes between actual absence and unknown presence.
3
Understand its implications
This objection weakens the argument that plants are not alive based on the sensation definition, because it leaves room for hidden sensations.
The objection does not prove plants have sensation, but shifts the burden of proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[قال (خاتمة)
النبات كما أنه ليس بحيوان، ليس بحي، لأن الحياة صفة تقتضي الحس، والحركة الإرادية، ومنهم من جعل التصرف في الغذاء حياة، فسماه حياً، ومنهم من بالغ فجعل للنبات مع الحس عقلاً].
لا خلاف في أن النبات ليس بحيوان لأن المراد به ما علم فيه تحقق الحس والحركة، وإنما الخلاف في حياته. فقيل هو حي، لأن الحياة صفة التغذية والتنمية . وقيل :لا : إذ الحياة صفة هي مبدأ الحس والحركة الإرادية . هي مبدأ واعترض بأنا لا نسلّم انتفاء ذلك في النبات غاية الأمر انتفاء العلم بتحققه فيه، ومنهم من ادعى تحققه فيه، متسشهداً بالأمارات على ما سبق، ومنهم من بلغ في اتصافه بالإدراك، حتى أثبت له إدراك الكليات وهو المعنى بالعقل، زعما منه، أن ما يشاهد من ميل إناث النخيل إلى بعض الذكور دون البعض، وميل عروقها إلى الصوب (۱) الذي فيه الماء، وانحرافها في صعودها عن الجدار المجاور، لا يتأتى بدون ذلك، وهذا ينسب إلى جمع من قدماء الحكماء.
[He said (Conclusion): The plant, just as it is not an animal, is not alive, because life is an attribute that necessitates sensation and voluntary movement. And among them is one who made the management of food life, so he called it alive. And among them is one who went so far as to attribute to the plant, along with sensation, intellect.] There is no disagreement that the plant is not an animal, because what is meant by animal is that in which sensation and movement are known to be realized. Rather the disagreement is concerning its life. So it is said: it is alive, because life is the attribute of nutrition and growth. And it is said: no, because life is an attribute that is the source of sensation and voluntary movement. It is the source. And it is objected: we do not concede the absence of that in the plant; at most it is the absence of knowledge of its realization in it. And among them is one who claimed its realization in it, adducing indications as mentioned earlier. And among them is one who went so far in attributing perception to it that he affirmed for it the perception of universals, which is the meaning of intellect, claiming that what is observed of the inclination of female date palms towards some males over others, and the inclination of their roots towards the rain (1) that contains water, and their deviation in climbing away from the adjacent wall, cannot occur without that. And this is attributed to a group of the ancient sages.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a concluding discussion on the status of plants. It begins by stating that plants are not animals and not alive if life is defined by sensation and voluntary movement. Some, however, define life as the ability to manage food, so they consider plants alive. Others go further, attributing both sensation and intellect to plants. The author then clarifies that there is no dispute that plants are not animals (since animals have sensation and voluntary movement), but the dispute is about whether they are alive. One opinion holds that life is the attribute of nutrition and growth, so plants are alive. Another opinion says life is the source of sensation and voluntary movement, so plants are not alive. An objection is raised: we do not concede that sensation and movement are absent in plants; we simply lack knowledge of their realization. Some have claimed that plants do have sensation and even intellect, using examples like female date palms turning toward specific male trees and roots turning toward water. They argue this could not occur without perception. This view is attributed to a group of ancient sages. The passage thus presents multiple views on plant life and perception, leaving the matter open.

Key Terms
الحس
الحركة الإرادية
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we don't observe sensation in plants, then they definitely don't have it.
Correction: The objection clarifies that lack of observation does not prove non-existence; it may simply be unknown.

Checkpoint
What is the core epistemological point raised in the objection?
A) Plants definitely have sensation
B) We cannot be sure plants have sensation because we don't see it
C) The absence of knowledge of sensation in plants does not prove its absence
D) Sensation in plants is impossible

Key Takeaway
Lack of evidence for a phenomenon does not constitute evidence against it—an important principle in theological reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a claim about the unseen that you cannot verify, do you tend to deny it outright or admit your ignorance? How does this lesson's principle of 'absence of proof is not proof of absence' challenge your default reaction? What would it cost you to remain humble about what you don't know?
?
Have you ever dismissed a valid objection to your beliefs simply because it made you uncomfortable or seemed like mere skepticism? This passage presents a genuine epistemological argument—are there areas in your faith where you have not fairly considered counterarguments?
?
The objector does not claim that plants have sensation, only that we cannot assume they don't. In your own life, where have you been too quick to conclude something is false because you lacked evidence? What might change if you admitted that uncertainty?

Ancient Sages on Plant Intellect: Perception of Universals
Presentation of the view that plants have intellect and the evidence cited from plant behavior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme view attributed to ancient sages that plants possess intellect, including the perception of universals.

Core Idea
Some ancient sages believed plants have intellect, reasoning from complex behaviors.

This lesson explores the most radical position presented: that plants not only have sensation but also intellect—the ability to perceive universal concepts. The passage provides examples of plant behaviors that were seen as evidence for this capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the extreme claim
Find where the text says 'some went so far as to attribute to the plant... intellect' and the claim of 'perception of universals'.
This is the strongest position on plant cognition.
2
Examine the examples
Look at the three examples: date palms, root orientation, and climbing away from a wall.
These behaviors are presented as requiring intellect.
3
Understand the reasoning
The sages argued that such goal-directed, selective behavior cannot occur without perception of universals.
They infer intellect from apparent choice and adaptation.

The Dispute on Plant Life: Definitions and Objections
Setting out the two definitions of life and an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scholars disagree on whether plants are alive, based on two different definitions of life.

Core Idea
Life can mean nutrition or sense-motion; plants fit one but not the other.

The author clarifies that the disagreement is not about whether plants are animals (they are not), but whether they qualify as 'living' given their observable functions. Some define life by nutrition and growth, which plants clearly have; others define it by sense and voluntary motion, which plants seem to lack. An important objection is then raised: we cannot be sure plants lack these; we only lack knowledge of them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two definitions of life
Read the Arabic and translation, then note the two definitions presented: one associates life with nutrition and growth, the other with sense and voluntary motion.
2
Understand the scope of the dispute
The passage emphasizes that there is no dispute that plants are not animals. The only dispute is about whether they are living creatures.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection states that just because we do not know whether plants have sense and motion, it does not mean they lack them. Consider the epistemological point being made.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحركة الإرادية، ومنهم من جعل التصرف في الغذاء حياة، فسماه حياً، ومنهم من بالغ فجعل للنبات مع الحس عقلاً. لا خلاف في أن النبات ليس بحيوان لأن المراد به ما علم فيه تحقق الحس والحركة، وإنما الخلاف في حياته. فقيل هو حي، لأن الحياة صفة التغذية والتنمية. وقيل: لا: إذ الحياة صفة هي مبدأ الحس والحركة الإرادية. واعترض بأنا لا نسلّم انتفاء ذلك في النبات غاية الأمر انتفاء العلم بتحققه فيه، ومنهم من ادعى تحققه فيه، مستشهداً بالأمارات على ما سبق، ومنهم من بلغ في اتصافه بالإدراك، حتى أثبت له إدراك الكليات وهو المعنى بالعقل، زعما منه، أن ما يشاهد من ميل إناث النخيل إلى بعض الذكور دون البعض، وميل عروقها إلى الصوب الذي فيه الماء، وانحرافها في صعودها عن الجدار المجاور، لا يتأتى بدون ذلك، وهذا ينسب إلى جمع من قدماء الحكماء.
and voluntary motion, and among them one who made the management of food life, so he called it living, and among them one who went so far as to attribute to plants, along with sense, intellect. There is no dispute that the plant is not an animal, because what is meant by animal is that in which sense and motion are known to be realized, and the dispute is only about its life. So it is said: it is living, because life is the quality of nutrition and growth. And it is said: no, because life is the quality that is the principle of sense and voluntary motion. And an objection was raised: we do not concede the absence of that in the plant; at most it is the absence of knowledge of its realization in it. And among them one who claimed its realization in it, adducing the indications as mentioned previously. And among them one who went so far in attributing perception to it, that he affirmed for it the perception of universals, which is what is meant by intellect, claiming that what is observed of the inclination of female palm trees toward some males rather than others, and the inclination of their roots toward the rain-bearing wind that contains water, and their deviation in their ascent away from the adjacent wall, cannot occur without that. And this is attributed to a group of ancient philosophers.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents the debate about whether plants possess life and perception. It begins by noting a range of opinions: some consider the management of food as life and thus call plants living; others go further and attribute both sense and intellect to plants. The author then clarifies that there is no disagreement that plants are not animals, since the definition of animal involves known sense and voluntary motion. The dispute is solely about the definition and attribution of life to plants. Two main positions are given: (1) plants are living because life is defined as the quality of nutrition and growth, which plants clearly have; (2) plants are not living because life is defined as the principle of sense and voluntary motion, which plants lack. The passage then records an objection to the second position: we cannot be certain that plants lack these attributes; we simply lack knowledge of them. Some scholars even claim that plants do have these attributes, citing observable evidence such as palm trees showing selective inclination (females toward specific males), roots growing toward water, and shoots avoiding adjacent walls. This, they argue, requires perception and even intellect (the perception of universals). The passage concludes by attributing this view to some ancient philosophers. The author is presenting these views without necessarily endorsing them, setting up the discussion for further analysis.

حس
حركة إرادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis of the objection to the view that plants are not alive?
A) Plants clearly show voluntary motion.
B) We cannot be sure that plants lack sense and motion; we only lack knowledge of them.
C) Nutrition and growth imply sense.
D) The definition of life is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The dispute on plant life hinges on whether life is defined by nutrition/growth or by sense/motion, and an epistemological objection warns against equating lack of knowledge with lack of existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What if you have assumed something is dead—like a spiritual faculty in yourself—simply because you haven't felt it? Will you now seek evidence with the same humility the objection demands?
?
The objection reminds us that absence of proof is not proof of absence. Where in your faith have you settled for doubt because you lacked immediate sensory evidence? Are you willing to admit you might be ignorant rather than conclude nothing is there?
?
The scholars disagreed on plants—things we treat as mere objects. How often do you treat other people, or even your own body, as merely mechanical, forgetting the life they may carry that you cannot measure? Will you change that today?

The Claim of Plant Intellect and Its Evidence
Presenting the evidence and argument for plant intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that plants possess intellect and sense, supported by observable behaviors.

Core Idea
Plants may perceive and even reason, based on their selective responses.

The passage continues with some scholars claiming that plants actually do have sense and perception, and even intellect (perception of universals). They cite examples like palm trees selecting specific males for pollination and roots seeking water as evidence that plants must have some form of awareness. This view is attributed to ancient philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the claim of intellect
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the specific behaviors cited as evidence: inclination of palm trees, root movement toward water, and avoidance of walls.
2
Identify the reasoning
Understand the argument: these selective behaviors cannot occur without some kind of perception, possibly even universal concepts (intellect).
3
Consider the source of this view
The passage ends by attributing this idea to ancient philosophers. Reflect on how this differs from mainstream Islamic thought.

Source Text

?
